Content key: BusinessDetailsPersonDetailsTaxFileNumber

Help content:

Tax File Number (TFN)

Do I have to provide my TFN?

If you're applying for an AUSkey, you must provide your TFN.

If you're not applying for an AUSkey, you don't have to provide your TFN. However, if you don't have a TFN or don't provide one, your application will be delayed by up to 28 days.

Why the delay?

Without a TFN, the Australian Business Register (ABR) may need to manually process your application. To do this, the ABR will need you to provide additional information or certified copies of documents including your:

  • bank account number
  • birth certificate
  • passport
  • driver?s licence.

Can I provide a TFN later?

If you don't have the TFN with you, you can leave the form and come back to it later. The form will be saved automatically.

As long as you don't lodge the form, you can return later and log in to resume your application from the Application summary screen.

Where can I find out more?

Details on what information the ABR needs to prove your identity can be found on the ABR website.

Content key: CompanyHolding

Help content:

Ultimate holding company

If your company won't have another company holding or controlling it, answer no to this question.

Your company has a holding company if another company holds most or all of your company's shares. Your company is 'held' or controlled by the holding company.

A holding company can in turn be held by another company, and so on. The ultimate holding company is the company at the top of the chain of ownership. It doesn?t have another company holding it.

Important: If you're not sure whether your company will have an ultimate holding company, consider seeking independent legal advice.

Content key: BusinessNameIntroText

Inline content:

Enter your proposed name to check if it's available to register as a business name.

You don't need to register a business name if it's the same as your company name. For example, if your company name is Digital Exports Pty Ltd and you want to carry on a business under this name, you don't need to register it as a business name.

If you want to operate under a different name, then you'll need to register it as a business name.

Read more about business names, trading names and legal names on business.gov.au.

Help content:

Proposed business name

Business name format

When you enter your business name, it will automatically be converted to uppercase letters. The system will also accept the following:

  • spaces
  • letters A to Z
  • numbers 0 to 9
  • standard punctuation marks such as . , ? ! ( ) { } : ; ? ? ?
  • the symbols | - _ \ / @ # $ % * = &

The form will not accept letters with an accent such as à, é, ĉ or ç.

Example: Lisa wants to register 'Lisa's Café'.

Although her name will need to be registered as 'LISA'S CAFE', she can still use 'Lisa's Café' on her marketing, signage and receipts.

Display your name

You are no longer required to display your business name registration certificate. However, legally you must display your business name on each:

  • location where you're trading to the public, such as shops or stalls at an exhibition
  • official documents, such as quotes, tax invoices and receipts.

Protect your name

Not all names are available to register. Names that are identical to a name registered by someone else, contain restricted or undesirable words or expressions can't be registered. Some names may also require manual processing. Check the business naming rules on the Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC) website to see what can and can't be registered.

Even if your proposed name meets the business naming rules, the same name could still be registered by someone else as a trade mark or domain name. Before you register your name, consider these steps:

  • Make sure you're not infringing on someone else's intellectual property (IP). Visit the IP Australia website for information on searching for trade marks in Australia and overseas for your proposed business name.
  • If you want to use your proposed business name as part of your web address, read our register a website name page.
  • Apply for a trade mark. Registering a business name doesn't stop others from using it for a different purpose. For example, even if you've registered a business name, others could still use the same name for their products. To protect your business name, apply for a trade mark on the IP Australia website.

Multiple names

You can register multiple business names. For example, if you run three businesses, you can register a different business name for each of them.

Renew a business name

The Business registration service does not currently process business name renewals. See the different options available for renewing your business name on the ASIC website.

Content key: SelectRegistrationsGST

Help content:

Goods & Services Tax (GST)

You must register for GST if:

  • your business has a GST turnover of $75,000 or more
  • your business charges passengers for taxi services or limousine travel (including ride sharing)
  • you want to claim Fuel Tax Credits (FTC)
  • you need to register for Wine Equalisation Tax (WET) or Luxury Car Tax (LCT).
Please note: You must have an Australian Business Number (ABN) in order to register for GST. If you don't already have an ABN, you can use this form to apply for both at the same time.

Don't expect to exceed $75,000? You can still volunteer to register for GST.

Registering for GST also allows you to claim credits for any GST included in the price of purchases for your business.

Visit the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) website to:

  • find out what GST turnover means
  • check if you need to register for GST, if you're not sure
  • see how GST applies to ride sharing services
  • find out more about claiming GST credits.

How GST works

GST is a tax of 10% on most goods and services sold in Australia. Generally, businesses that are registered for GST:

  • collect GST on the goods and services they sell and forward it to the ATO
  • claim credits for the GST included in the price of goods and services they buy for their business.

What do I need to do?

If you're registered for GST, you'll need to:

  • work out which goods and services you sell are subject to GST
  • include GST in the prices you charge for those goods and services
  • give tax invoices to your customers that include the GST amount
  • account for GST in your books
  • put aside the GST you've collected
  • get tax invoices and claim GST credits for goods and services you buy for your own business
  • lodge activity statements or annual returns to the ATO
  • pay GST to the ATO (or get a GST refund).

Find out more

Read about how GST works on the ATO website.

Content key: SelectRegistrationsCompany

Inline content: $479+

Help content:

Australian company

A company is a type of business structure. When you set up a company, you create a legal entity that's separate from you. To find out more about companies and how they're different from other business structures, visit our company page.

Company registration

To set up a company, it must be registered under the Corporations Act 2001.

You can register these types of companies here:

  • Proprietary limited companies (also known as private companies) - the most common company structure used by small businesses
  • Public companies limited by shares or guarantee
  • Public companies with no liability
  • Special purpose companies (not for profit, home unit/body corporate, superannuation trustee)

To register a private or public company that's unlimited (where the liability of shareholders is unlimited), you need to apply:

  • by using the company registration form on the Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC) website, or
  • through a business service provider.

To find out more about these application options, see step 6 of how to start a company on the ASIC website.

Fees

The fees you pay depend on the type of company you're registering. Visit the ASIC website for details of:

  • the one-off fee for company registration (see starting a company - common fees)
  • the annual fee for company reviews.

Before you register

Before you register a company, you need to:

  • choose a company name
  • decide whether your company will be proprietary or public
  • work out who the directors and shareholders will be
  • understand your legal obligations as a director and shareholder.

To find out more, read company officeholder duties on the ASIC website.

After you register

When registered, the company will:

  • get a unique Australian Company Number (ACN) and a Certificate of Registration
  • appear in the company register
  • be required to complete an annual review and pay an annual review fee each year (see annual statements on the ASIC website)
  • be required to tell ASIC of changes in company details, usually within 28 days of the change taking place (otherwise, late fees may apply - see check and change company details on the ASIC website).

To find out what company information is available to the public in the company register, go to companies and organisations - search ASIC's Registers.

For information about how ASIC handles personal information, read the summary of ASIC's privacy policy.

If the TAN number you find is only 7 digits, add a '0' at the beginning of your number to complete this field.

The start date of your FTC registration is the date you became eligible to claim FTC.

The date must be:

  • the same as or later than your GST start date
  • earlier than your ABN end date (if there is one)

  • no earlier than 4 years in the past.

To make sure you're eligible for FTC, try the FTC eligibility tool on the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) website.

Content key: TaxationGstDetailsGstTurnover

Help content:

GST turnover

GST turnover is your current or projected annual turnover (excluding GST amounts) ? whichever is greater. GST turnover is not your profit.

Current annual turnover is:

  • the value of all sales you made or are likely to make in the current month plus
  • the value of all sales you made in the previous 11 months.

Projected annual turnover is:

  • the value of all sales you made or are likely to make in the current month plus
  • the value of all sales you're likely to make in the next 11 months.

If you need more help working out your annual GST turnover visit the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) website.

Content key: TaxationGstDetailsRegistrationDate

Inline content: Must be your ABN start date or later.

Help content:

GST start date

If you're required by law to register for GST, your GST registration should start on the date:

  • your annual turnover reached $75,000 or more
  • you started charging passengers for taxi services or limousine travel (including ride sharing)
  • you want to start claiming fuel tax credits.

If you choose to register for GST, you can choose any start date, as long as it's the same as or later than your ABN start date.

Important: You?re expected to stay registered for at least 12 months. If you apply for your GST registration to be cancelled within 12 months, you may be refused or be required to report monthly.

Visit the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) website to:

  • find out more about GST
  • see how GST applies to ride sharing services
  • work out how you can claim GST credits.

Content key: TaxationGstDetailsAccountingMethodCashYesNo

Help content:

Cash versus Accrual accounting method

Cash accounting method

Cash accounting tracks the actual money coming in and out of your business.

Example of cash accounting
If you send an invoice on Tuesday, and receive the payment in your account on Thursday, you record the income against Thursday's date in your books.

You're using cash accounting if you account for GST on a sale when you receive payment (not when you issue the invoice).

You can choose the cash accounting method for GST if:

  • your annual turnover is less than $2 million and you?re a small business
  • you're accounting for your income using the cash method for income tax purposes.

If you don't meet any of the criteria above and want to use cash accounting for GST, this may be possible. Contact the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) to seek permission.

Accrual accounting method

If you use accrual accounting, you record expenses and sales when they take place, instead of when cash changes hands.

Example of accrual accounting
If you're a builder and have sent an invoice for a project you've completed, you record the sale in your books even though you haven't received payment yet.

You're using an accruals or non-cash accounting method if you account for GST on a sale when you issue an invoice or receive any part of the payment - whichever happens first.

Need more help with your finances?

Get started by visiting the Finance & accounting topic on the business.gov.au website.

Content key: TaxationGstDetailsImportingGoodsYesNo

Help content:

Importing goods into Australia

If you import goods into Australia, you may be eligible for the Deferred GST Scheme. The scheme allows you to defer payment of GST on imports. To be in the scheme, you must lodge your activity statements online every month.

Content key: TaxationGstDetailsRequiredToRegisterYesNo

Help content:

Goods and Services Tax (GST) registration

When registration is legally required

You?re required by law to register for GST if you:

  • have GST turnover of $75,000 or more ($150,000 for charities)
  • charge passengers for taxi services
  • charge passengers for limousine travel, or
  • want to claim fuel tax credits.

When you choose to register

If you?re not required by law to register for GST, you can still choose to register. For example, if you expect that you will soon have turnover of $75,000 or more.

Important: You?re expected to stay registered for at least 12 months. If you apply for your GST registration to be cancelled within 12 months, you may be refused.

Main business activity

Your main business activity is usually the activity that earns you the most money or is closest to the purpose of your business. For example:

  • café
  • wine wholesaling
  • real estate services
  • web hosting
  • accounting services.
Please note: Other government agencies may contact you about important information relevant to your business activity.

If you have additional business activities and they apply to a different address, you can add them separately in the 'Additional addresses' section of this form.

Content key: EntitlementCompanyTypeLegend

Inline content: Type of company

Content key: EntitlementCompanyTypeAPTY

Inline content: Proprietary companies must have at least one director and one shareholder. They can raise funds from existing shareholders and employees. They generally aren't allowed to raise funds by selling shares to the public.

Help content:

Proprietary (private) company

When registering a company, most businesses in Australia choose to register a proprietary company.

Proprietary companies are also known as private companies. They:

  • can have up to 50 non-employee shareholders
  • can raise funds from existing shareholders and employees
  • can't be listed on a share market
  • generally aren't allowed to raise money from the public by selling shares (like public companies can)
  • must have at least one shareholder and one director.

You can be both a shareholder and a director. You can also be a 'one personal company', where you are the only shareholder and director.

If you're not sure whether a private or public company structure best suits your business, consider getting advice from an independent professional.

Content key: EntitlementCompanySubclassLegend

Inline content: Sub-type of company

Content key: EntitlementEntitySubTypePUB

Inline content: Public companies can sell shares to the public to raise funds They can have an unlimited number of shareholders.

Help content:

Public company

Public companies can have an unlimited number of non-employee shareholders, and can raise funds by selling shares to the public.

Public companies include:

  • listed companies
  • non-profit companies
  • statutory corporations and their subsidiaries
  • friendly society dispensary companies
  • mutual life assurance companies.

Content key: EntitlementEntitySubTypePRV

Inline content: Private companies can't raise funds from the public by selling shares. They must have 50 or fewer shareholders.

Help content:

Private company

Private companies (also known as proprietary companies) can not be listed on a share market.

They must have 50 or fewer non-employee shareholders and can raise funds from existing shareholders and employees. They generally aren?t allowed to raise money from the public by selling shares (like public companies can).

Content key: EntitlementEntitySubTypeCOP

Inline content: Co-operatives are owned and controlled by members, and operate for the benefit of their members. For example, food co-ops selling food at a lower price to members.

Help content:

Co-operative

A co-operative:

  • operates for the sole benefit of its members
  • is owned and controlled by its members
  • is registered with the relevant state or territory authority
  • has rules that set out what activities it can carry out.

A co-operative is a legal entity separate from its individual members. It can enter into legal contracts (such as a lease), sue and be sued in its own name.

Co-operatives generally fall into four categories:

  • Consumer ? buying and then selling goods to members at lower rates (such as food co-ops)
  • Marketing ? branding and distributing the products or services of members
  • Service ? providing services to members, such as health, electricity or housing
  • Community ? sharing resources, information and skills.

Co-operatives can only operate in the state or territory it?s registered in.

Content key: EntitlementEntitySubTypeOIE

Inline content: Organisations that are like companies but not incorporated under the Corporations Act 2001 (such as foreign companies and associations incorporated under state acts).

Help content:

Other incorporated entity

Other incorporated entities are organisations that are like companies but not incorporated under the Corporations Act 2001, such as:

  • a branch of an overseas company not incorporated in Australia (often the name ends in corporation)
  • associations which are incorporated under a State or Territory Act
  • body corporates
  • corporations sole
  • incorporated charitable institutions.

Content key: EntitlementEntitySubTypeSTR

Inline content: Where a title to a property is divided into a number of units for sale.

Help content:

Strata title

A strata title is where a title to a property is divided into a number of units for sale. Sometimes, parts of the property are kept as common areas for all unit holders to use (such as gardens, foyers, driveways).

A strata title must:

  • have a strata corporation. This is the legal body that maintains common areas and looks after the property for its members.
  • register with the relevant state or territory authority.

A strata corporation may be called something else, depending on the state or territory the property is in and the type of strata scheme. Other names for strata corporation include:

  • owners? corporation
  • body corporate
  • strata company
  • community association

Content key: EntitlementEntitySubTypeCUT

Inline content: Corporate unit trusts hold a business or property transferred from a company.

Help content:

Corporate unit trust

Where a company runs or owns a business or property, a corporate unit trust is set up when:

  • the business or property transfers to a unit trust, and
  • the shareholders of the company are entitled to take up units in the unit trust, and
  • the unit trust is either a resident unit trust or was a corporate unit trust in a previous income year.

Corporate unit trusts must:

  • report as a company for income tax purposes
  • lodge a company tax return instead of a trust return.

Content key: EntitlementEntitySubTypePTT

Inline content: Trusts created to finance or get an interest in the operation or development of a business or project.

Help content:

Public trading trust

This is a trust created to finance or get an interest in the operation or development of a business or project (not to invest in a project).

Public trading trusts must:

  • report as a company for income tax purposes
  • lodge a company tax return instead of a trust return.

Content key: EntitlementEntitySubTypeUIE

Inline content: Organisations that are formed by a number of people for a common purpose and that aren't registered with any government authorities.

Help content:

Other unincorporated entity

An organisation is an unincorporated entity if it is:

  • a number of people coming together for a common purpose without setting up a legal structure (such as a sporting club or social club)
  • not registered with state or territory or Commonwealth authorities.

Because the organisation isn?t a separate legal entity, it can't enter into contracts in its own name, or employ people, or sue or be sued. The members of the organisation do these things on behalf of the organisation.

Content key: EntitlementEntitySubTypeLPT

Inline content: Partnerships made up of at least one general partner and one limited partner.

Help content:

Limited partnership

Limited partnerships are where the partners don't equally share the partnership. They are often formed because at least one partner wants to limit their liability.

Your limited partnership needs to be registered with the relevant Consumer affairs or Fair trading office in your state or territory.

Limited partnerships must:

  • report as a company for income tax purposes
  • lodge a company tax return instead of a partnership return.

If you're not sure about something to do with your limited partnership, please seek independent legal advice.

Content key: TaxationLctDetailsRegistrationDate

Inline content: Must be your GST start date or later.

Help content:

LCT start date

This is the date you must start paying LCT. Generally, you must start paying LCT if:

  • you're registered (or must register) for GST, and
  • you sell or import a luxury car.

The LCT start date must be:

  • the same as or later than your GST start date
  • before your ABN end date (if there is one)
  • no later than 6 months in the future
  • no earlier than 4 years in the past.

Find out more about when LCT applies.

WET start date

This is the date you were required to start paying WET.

If you make wine, import wine into Australia or sell it by wholesale, you normally have to pay WET.

The start date must be:

  • the same as or later than your GST start date
  • earlier than your ABN end date (if there is one)
  • no later than 6 months in the future
  • no earlier than 4 years in the past.

Find out more about when you have to pay WET.

Content key: TaxationPaygDetailsRegistrationDate

Inline content:

Can't be before your ABN start date.

Help content:

Pay As You Go (PAYG) withholding

PAYG withholding start date

Your PAYG withholding registration start date must:

  • be before you start withholding amounts
  • not be before your ABN start date
  • be within the next 6 months.

When do I withhold PAYG?

You must withhold PAYG if you:

  • have employees
  • have a voluntary agreement with non-employees to withhold from your payments to them
  • make payments to businesses that don't quote their Australian business number (ABN).

PAYG withholding isn't a tax on your business - it refers to the amounts you're required to withhold from payments to your employees and other workers.

What do I need to do?

Being registered for PAYG withholding means you:

  • work out how much you need to withhold
  • report PAYG withholding amounts to the ATO
  • pay PAYG withholding amounts to the ATO when they're due
  • include PAYG withholding amounts in your employees' pay slips and payment summaries.

Find out more

Read about PAYG withholding on the ATO website.

Content key: TaxationPaygDetailsEstimatedNumberOfPayees

Help content:

Employees

This helps the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) work out the number of payment summaries required.

When working out how many employees you have, don't confuse your employees with contractors! Contractors set up businesses to hire out their services to other businesses, and can negotiate their own working arrangements.

Generally, employees:

  • are supervised by an employer
  • are required to carry out their work in a particular way
  • earn a wage or salary
  • are entitled to paid leave
  • work set hours on a permanent, fixed term or casual basis
  • can't subcontract work tasks given to them.

If you're still not sure whether you have an employee or a contractor, try our Independent contractors decision tool.

Content key: TaxationPaygDetailsEstimatedTaxWithheldAmount

Help content:

Expected amount of withholding

This is only an estimate and helps the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) work out how often you'll be required to report and pay withholding amounts.

You will be required to pay:

  • quarterly if you withhold $25 000 or less
  • monthly if you withhold between $25 001 and $1 000 000 (inclusive)
  • more frequently if you withhold more than $1 000 000

Find details on the Payments you need to withhold from on the ATO website.

To help you calculate the amount you need to withhold, use the ATO's Tax withheld calculator

Content key: TaxationPaygDetailsReportingMethod

Help content:

Payment summary annual report

At the end of each year you need to send the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) an annual report summarising all payments and amounts withheld for that year.

The report must include:

  • all payments you made to employees, workers or businesses during the financial year
  • the amounts you withheld from those payments.

To find out more on what you need to provide in the annual report visit the PAYG withholding annual reporting page on the ATO website.

Content key: TaxationPaygDetailsInvestmentBodyYesNo

Help content:

Royalties, dividends, interest or investment body

You must be registered for PAYG withholding if you:

  • pay royalties, dividends or interest to non-residents
  • are an investment body that pays investment income to Australian residents.

To find out more about your PAYG withholding obligations, visit the PAYG Withholding page on the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) website.

Content key: TaxationFbtDetailsRegistrationDate

Inline content:

Can't be before your ABN start date.

Help content:

Fringe Benefits Tax (FBT)

FBT start date

You must register for FBT once you have determined you're providing fringe benefits. Your FBT registration start date must:

  • not be before your ABN start date
  • be within the next 6 months.

What are fringe benefits?

Examples of common fringe benefits include offering:

  • a work car for private use
  • cheap loans
  • free private health insurance
  • food or drink, such as meals and cocktail parties
  • entertainment such as sporting events, sightseeing tours, movie tickets and harbour cruises
  • low interest or interest-free loans
  • accommodation rent-free or at a reduced rent.

What do I need to do?

Being registered for FBT means you:

  • work out how much FBT you have to pay
  • keep records related to the fringe benefits you provide
  • report fringe benefits on your employees' payment summaries
  • report fringe benefits to the Australian Taxation Office (ATO)
  • pay the FBT to the ATO.

Find out more

Read about Fringe Benefits Tax on the ATO website.

Content key: BusinessDetailsReasonForApplicationNewBusinessYesNo

Help content:

First time in business (New business)

Answer 'Yes' to this question if you've never owned or operated a business in Australia. This includes as a sole trader, partner, trustee, director or contractor/subcontractor.

Content key: BusinessDetailsReasonForApplicationBoughtExisting

Inline content: You have bought or taken over an existing business.

Content key: TaxationLctDetailsAnnualLiability

Help content:

Estimate of LCT you will pay in the next 12 months

Give a rough estimate of the LCT amount you think you'll need to pay in the next 12 months. LCT is 33% of the value of a luxury car above the LCT threshold.

As a guide, here's how to work out the LCT amount if you sell or import a car:

    (LCT value ? LCT threshold) x 10/11 x 33%

The LCT value generally includes:

  • any GST, and
  • the value of any parts, accessories or attachments you supplied or imported at the same time as the car.

Visit the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) website to find out more about:

  • working out the LCT on an import
  • working out the LCT on a sale.

Why do I need to provide an estimate?

The ATO uses your estimate to prefill the LCT amount payable on your first BAS (business activity statement) form before they send it to you.

The BAS form is for you to report and pay LCT, along with any other tax registrations.

If you need to, you can change the LCT amount on the BAS form based on your actual sales or imports for that period.

Content key: TaxationLctDetailsMainActivityPercentage

Help content:

Percentage of luxury car turnover

For example, say you have luxury car turnover of $10 million.

If $2.5 million is from importing luxury cars and $7.5 million is from manufacturing luxury cars, you would enter:

  • 25% for Importing
  • 75% for Manufacturing
  • 0% for Retailing
  • 0% for Wholesaling
Please note: When we say turnover, we mean your gross income from luxury cars (not your profit).

The Australian Taxation Office asks this question to help them understand what types of businesses are registering for LCT.

Content key: HowToProveIdentity

Inline content: Providing your TFN is the quickest way to prove your identity.

Content key: TaxationLctDetailsOnEveryActivityStatementYesNo

Help content:

Reporting LCT on your BAS

You'll need to report LCT to the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) on your BAS, along with any other business taxes you're registered for.

The ATO will adjust the BAS forms they send out to you, based on your answer to this question.

Find out more about reporting and keeping LCT records on the ATO website.

Content key: TaxationFctDetailsHeavyVehicleYesNo

Help content:

Fuel used in vehicles to travel on public roads

The Australian Taxation Office (ATO) asks this question to help them understand how fuels are used in businesses.

GVM (gross vehicle mass) is the maximum weight a vehicle can carry, including its own weight. It includes the weight of the chassis, body, engine, fuel, accessories, driver, passengers, and load. GVM is set by the manufacturer.

If you travel on public roads for business reasons:

  • you can claim FTC for fuel used in a heavy vehicle (GVM greater than 4.5 tonnes)
  • you can?t claim FTC for fuel used in a light vehicle (GVM of 4.5 tonnes or less).

Find out more about road transport and FTC on the ATO website.

Content key: BusinessActivityIsResidentOfAustralia

Help content:

Resident for tax purposes

Individuals (sole traders)

We need to know if you're a resident for tax purposes to work out if you're entitled to an Australian Business Number (ABN).

If you've recently arrived in Australia and you're not sure if you're a resident, take the are you a resident test.

Companies

A company incorporated in Australia is a resident in Australia for tax purposes.

A company not incorporated in Australia is a resident in Australia for tax purposes if:

  • it carries on business in Australia and
  • its central management and control is in Australia or its voting power is controlled by shareholders who are residents of Australia.

To work out whether individual shareholders are Australian residents for tax purposes, use the are you a resident test on the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) website.

Partnerships

A corporate limited partnership is a resident of Australia if either:

  • the partnership was formed in Australia, or
  • the partnership either carries on business in Australia, or has its central management and control in Australia.

Trusts

Trusts other than unit trusts are considered Australian residents in any given income year if either:

  • a trustee of the trust estate was a resident at any time during the income year, or
  • the central management and control of the trust estate was in Australia at any time during the income year.

Unit trusts will be an Australian resident unit trust if at any time during the income year:

  • one of these requirements was satisfied:
    • any property of the unit trust was situated in Australia, or
    • the trustee of the unit trust carried on a business in Australia.
  • and one of these requirements was satisfied:
    • the central management and control of the unit trust was in Australia, or
    • one or more Australian residents held more than 50% of the beneficial interests in the income or property of the trust.

If you aren't sure if your trust meets the residency requirements go to the ATO website.

Superannuation funds

A superannuation fund is an Australian resident superannuation fund in any given income year if:
  • it is established in Australia (or has an asset in Australia at the relevant time)
  • has its central control and management in Australia
  • has at least one active member and at least 50% of the benefits of the active members of the fund are held for members who are Australian residents.

Self Managed Superannuation Funds (SMSFs)

To be a complying SMSF and receive tax concessions, your fund needs to be an Australian resident regulated super fund at all times during the income year.

If a member moves or travels overseas for an extended period, this may affect the residency status of the fund. Your fund needs to meet certain conditions to be an ?Australian superannuation fund?.

Read more about SMSF residency conditions on the ATO website.

Content key: TaxationWetDetailsBeveragesTurnoverPercentage

Help content:

Percentage of alcoholic beverages turnover

For example, say you have alcoholic beverages turnover of $10 million.

If $2.5 million is from selling wine and $7.5 million is from selling spirits, you would enter 25% for Wine and 75% for Spirits.

Note: When we say turnover, we mean your gross income from alcoholic beverages (not your profit).

The Australian Taxation Office asks this question to help them better understand the types of the businesses that are paying WET.

Content key: BusinessActivityOperateAnAgriculturalPropertyYesNo

Help content:

Agricultural property

This includes land used to breed, keep, cultivate or grow all kinds of animals, crops, fruit or vegetables.

This doesn't include land used for forest trees or marine life.

Content key: CompanyDetailsAllocateSharesLegend

Inline content: Give details of the shares that each shareholder has agreed to take up. The company must have agreement from the shareholder in writing.

Content key: CompanyDetailsAllocateSharesShareClassCode

Help content:

Share class

Select the share class that the shareholder has agreed in writing to take up.

What is a share class?

A company can use different classes of shares to give shareholders different rights (such as different voting powers or different dividend rates).

To keep it simple, most companies make all their shares ordinary shares. The ordinary share class is the most commonly used share class. Ordinary shares give shareholders normal voting and dividend rights.

Important! If you?re not sure what share class to use, consider getting advice from a business adviser or other professional.

Other standard share classes

Other standard share classes include:

  • A to F class shares - these shares usually give dividends at different rates compared to ordinary shareholders
  • preference shares - these shares usually give holders preference in some things, such as priority payment of dividends over other share classes
  • redeemable shares - depending on their terms of issue, these shares can usually be redeemed at the decision of the company, the shareholder, after a fixed time, or on a specific date.

While these share classes are standard, each company can decide on the rights and restrictions attached to them. For example, two companies may issue class A shares, but the rights attached to these shares can be completely different.

Custom share class

If the company uses a custom share class, you must provide the following details:

  • Class code - made up of 1 to 4 letters.
  • Class description - a short phrase that describes the share class.

For example, a family-held company has created a special class of shares for family members, which gives an extra dividend based on their contribution to the company. The custom share class code is FAM and the class description is family special dividend.

Find out more

Find out more about company shares on the Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC) website.

Content key: CompanyDetailsAllocateSharesIsBeneficiallyHeld

Help content:

Shares beneficially held

Most shareholders beneficially hold shares. This means the holder of the shares gets the direct benefit from the shares (such as dividend payments).

For example, Paul is the sole director and shareholder of the company Paul Enterprises PTY LTD.

He receives all the benefits from the company in the form of dividends, which means all his shares are beneficially held.

You don't beneficially hold shares if you hold them:

  • as a trustee (the beneficiaries of the trust get the direct benefit of the shares, not you)
  • as a nominee (the person who has nominated you to hold the shares gets the direct benefit of the shares, not you)
  • on behalf of another person (the person you hold them on behalf of gets the direct benefit, not you).

Content key: CompanyDetailsAllocateSharesCustomShareClassCode

Inline content: 1 to 4 letters

Content key: CompanyDetailsAllocateSharesCustomShareClassDescription

Inline content: Short phrase that describes the share class

Content key: SelectRegistrationsAUSkey

Help content:

Business logins - AUSkey

Only Administrator AUSkey registration is available here.

What is an AUSkey?

An AUSkey is a secure login that's set up on your computer. If you're eligible, AUSkey lets you log in to many government services including business and tax forms.

What can I do with an AUSkey?

With a compatible system or browser, an AUSkey can help you:

  • update your business name details through ASIC connect
  • lodge your Business Activity Statements (BAS) through the Business Portal
  • update your Australian Business Number (ABN) details
  • submit tax statements through 'SBR-enabled' accounting, bookkeeping or payroll desktop software.

See where you can use your AUSkey on the Australian Business Register (ABR) website.

If you want to log in from different computers, you can set up an AUSkey on a USB stick.

How do I register?

You can only register for an Administrator AUSkey here by selecting the AUSkey checkbox together with another registration on the 'Ready to register' screen. All you need to provide is your:

  • Tax file number
  • Australian telephone number
  • Email address.

Find out more about registering for an AUSkey on the ABR website.

To register a tax agent or accountant for a Standard AUSkey, you'll need to register on the Australian Business Register (ABR) website.

You can now choose how you want to log in to manage your government business and tax details. Only AUSkey is available to register here on this Business registration form. To find out more about additional business login options, expand the "Additional logins" tab below.

Additional logins

You can now choose how you want to log in to manage your government business and tax details.

You can use your AUSkey or one of the login options below:

  • Manage ABN connections
    If you have a MyGov login, you can link your ABNs to your MyGov account. This means you can then use your MyGov account to manage your ABN details.
  • Online or 'cloud' tax software
    You can now use eligible online or 'cloud' accounting, bookkeeping or payroll software to process certain government tax forms.

Please note: the login options above are for specific tax paperwork. You'll still need to use either your AUSkey or ASIC connect login to:

  • manage business name details or renewals
  • submit tax forms through 'SBR-enabled' accounting, bookkeeping or payroll desktop software.

Visit the MyGov website to find out more about the Manage ABN Connections option.

Find out more about the online or 'cloud' software service on the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) website.

Content key: CompanyDetailsContactDetailsStates

Help content:

State or territory of company registration

Once you register a company, it can operate in any state or territory. Company registration is a national registration.

However, you still need to choose the state or territory the company is 'taken to be registered in'. Most companies choose the state or territory in which they will operate their company.

The company will need to meet any laws of that state or territory that apply to it. For example, stamp duty rates vary between the states and territories.

The state or territory will appear on the certificate of company registration.

You can apply to change the state or territory of registration later, but there is a fee to do this. See Application for change of state or territory of registration of a company on the Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC) website for details.

Content key: BusinessDetailsAuthorisedContactDetailsLegend

Inline content: You need to provide at least one authorised contact who can be contacted for queries about this application and your business registrations.

You can nominate yourself as the authorised contact, but if you nominate a tax agent, you should also provide their tax agent number.

Help content:

Authorised contact

You can choose to nominate one or more people to be authorised contacts for your business name, ABN and tax registrations.

For ABN or tax registration, an authorised contact can ask the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) to change your details on your behalf. Find out more about ATO authorised contacts on the ATO website.

For business name registration, an authorised contact can complete transactions with the Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC) on your behalf. Find out more about Authorised lodgers for business names on the ASIC website.

An authorised contact can't add, remove or update your authorised contacts unless they declare they've been given authority to do so by you.

You can nominate any individual to become your authorised contact, as long as they meet POI (proof of identity) requirements. Authorised contacts can include:

  • registered tax agents
  • registered BAS agents
  • lawyers
  • guardians
  • family members or friends.

Content key: CompanyDetailsContactDetailsIsPrincipalPlaceOfBusiness

Inline content: Will the address be the main place of business?

Help content:

Main place of business

The 'main place of business' is where the company's main activity will take place. It must be a street address (not a post box).

The address will be available to the public in the company register for a small fee. In the register, it's called the 'principal place of business'.

It's up to you to decide what address to use. For example, if the company will:

  • run a retail store or a professional practice, you could use the street address of this location
  • be run from a director's home, you could use their residential address (make sure the company has written consent)
  • conduct its business online or at its customers' premises, you could use the street address at which the company books and records are kept.
  • have multiple business locations, you could use the head office.

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessStructureNotSure

Inline content: Help me decide.

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessStructureSoleTrader

Inline content: You're an individual and the sole owner of the business, but you can employ staff. You're responsible for all decisions and losses in your business, and you report the business income in your personal tax return.

Help content:

Sole traders

A sole trader structure is the easiest and cheapest business structure to set up.

If you're a sole trader, you're an individual and the sole owner of the business.

The income your business makes belongs to you. You report the income on your individual tax return and pay tax on your business income at your individual income tax rate.

You're responsible for all decisions and losses in your business. This means you have complete control over your business, but it also means your personal assets are at risk if things go wrong.

If you employ staff, you're responsible for giving them their minimum employee entitlements and conditions (such as superannuation and leave).

Example of a sole trader

Jason operates his landscaping business as a sole trader, he trades on his own and controls and manages the business. He employs a part-time junior gardener to help out on basic garden maintenance jobs.

The business income is treated as Jason's individual income and he is solely responsible for any tax the business must pay. He includes all his business income with any other income and reports it on his individual tax return.

Key facts for sole traders

  • Cost - cheapest structure to set up and run
  • Setting up process - easy
  • Owner - you
  • Responsibility for business decisions - you
  • Responsibility for losses - you
  • Report business income - on your individual tax return
  • Tax rate - you pay your individual tax rate on any business profit
  • Risk of losing assets - yes
  • Business tax file number (TFN) needed - no
  • Additional administration - no
  • Additional reporting - no
  • Separate business bank account needed - no
  • Your super - you're not considered an employee, so you're responsible for organising your own superannuation

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessStructurePartnership

Inline content: You and your partners carry on a business together and share income, losses, and control of the business. The partnership can employ staff, but the partners in the partnership are not considered employees.

Help content:

Partnerships

A partnership structure is relatively inexpensive and easy to set up and operate.

In a partnership structure, you and your business partners own the business together and share the income and losses of the business. A partnership can have two or more partners.

You're in a family partnership structure if two or more of the partners are related.

Each partner reports their share of the income on their own individual tax return, and pays tax at their individual income tax rate. The partnership must lodge a partnership income tax return every year with the Australian Taxation Office (ATO).

Tax File Number (TFN): A partnership needs its own TFN. The good news is you don't need to register. The ATO will automatically issue a partnership with a TFN (if one doesn't already exist).

All partners share legal responsibility for the business. This means the personal assets of all partners are at risk if things go wrong. If some partners want to limit their responsibility for business losses, you might want to consider a limited partnership structure. See below for more information.

If your business employs staff, all partners are responsible for giving staff their minimum employee entitlements and conditions (such as superannuation and leave).

Example of a partnership

Greg and Fiona operate their website business as a partnership. The partnership has its own tax file number (TFN).

A partnership is not a separate legal entity and doesn't pay income tax on the income it earns. Instead, Greg and Fiona pay tax on their share of the net partnership income they each receive.

While the partnership doesn't pay tax, it does have to lodge an annual partnership tax return to show all income the partnership earned and deductions it claimed for expenses it incurred in carrying on the partnership business. The tax return also shows each partner's share of the net partnership income.

Key facts for partnerships

  • Cost - low cost to set up and run
  • Setting up process - relatively easy
  • Owner - you and your partners
  • Responsibility for business decisions - you and your partners share
  • Responsibility for losses - you and your partners share
  • Report business income - each partner reports their share in their individual tax return
  • Tax rate - all partners pay their individual tax rate on their share of any business profit
  • Risk of losing assets - yes
  • Additional administration - if partners' shares aren't equal, a partnership agreement is required
  • Business tax file number (TFN) needed - yes
  • Additional reporting - yes, an annual partnership return must be lodged at the ATO under the business TFN
  • Separate business bank account needed - yes
  • Your superannuation - you're not considered an employee, so you're responsible for organising your own superannuation.

Limited partnerships

In a limited partnership, there are two types of partners: a general partner and a limited partner. There must be at least one of each.

General partners are responsible for the day-to-day management of the business and their responsibility for losses (their liability for debt) is unlimited. Limited partners don't play any role in the day-to-day management of the business, and their liability for debt is limited to the amount they contributed to the partnership.

You must register a limited partnership in your state or territory. Contact your state or territory government to find out more.

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessStructureCompany

Inline content: A company is a separate legal entity from you, and has at least one shareholder (owner) and one director (who runs the business). Income and losses belong to the company. Companies can employ staff.

Help content:

Companies

A company structure has higher costs and is more difficult to set up. Running a company can also be more expensive, because you have to meet reporting and other requirements under Australian company law. This is why the structure is generally considered to be better suited to medium to large businesses.

A company structure means that your business is a separate legal entity from you. This means the company has the same rights as a natural person and can have debt, sue and be sued. A company is owned by its shareholders and run by its directors.

The personal assets of directors and shareholders are separate from the company, so they can't be used to pay company debts. In certain cases where directors breach their duties, they may be held personally responsible for company losses.

The income and losses that the business makes belongs to the company, and is reported on a company tax return. The company tax rate applies to the business income.

Tax File Number (TFN): A company needs its own TFN. The good news is you don't need to register. The ATO will automatically issue a company with a TFN (if one doesn't already exist).

If the company employs staff, the company is responsible for giving them their minimum employee entitlements and conditions (such as superannuation and leave).

Companies must appoint a public officer and let the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) know. Public officers are responsible for making sure the company complies with tax law, keeping tax records and submitting company tax returns. The ATO needs to know their details so they can contact them about company tax matters.

Proprietary limited company

Companies can be proprietary (private) or public. Most companies in Australia are proprietary limited. A proprietary limited company generally isn't allowed to raise money from the public by selling shares (like public companies can).

They must have:

  • no more than 50 non-employee shareholders
  • at least one shareholder (also known as a member)
  • at least one director.

You can be both a shareholder and a director. You can also be the sole shareholder and director.

When companies are 'limited', the liability of shareholders is limited to any unpaid value (if any) on their shares. This means that shareholders can only be responsible for company losses up to any amount unpaid on their shares.

Visit the Australian Securities & Commissions (ASIC) website to find out more about:

  • shareholders (members)
  • company officeholders (directors and secretaries)
  • duties of company officeholders.

Example of a company

Mary runs her business as a company and the money the business earns belongs to the company. The company must have a separate bank account.

Companies have to lodge an annual company tax return, which shows the company's income, deductions and income tax it is liable to pay.

Public companies

Unlike private companies, public companies can have an unlimited number of non-employee shareholders and raise money from the public by selling shares. A public company must have at least one shareholder, one secretary, and three directors.

Key facts for companies

  • Cost - higher cost to set up and run
  • Setting up process - more complicated
  • Owner - company shareholders
  • Responsibility for business decisions - the director(s)
  • Responsibility for losses - generally, the company is responsible
  • Report business income - on the company tax return
  • Other income - if you're a shareholder and/or director, you pay your individual tax rate on any share dividends and/or your earnings as a director
  • Tax rate - the company pays tax on any profits at the company tax rate
  • Risk of losing assets - if you're a director, you can be personally liable in some cases for company debts
  • Business Tax file number (TFN) needed - yes
  • Additional administration - yes, registration with ASIC and reporting and lodgement requirements
  • Additional reporting - yes, annual company tax return and other requirements
  • Separate business bank account needed - yes
  • Your superannuation - if you're a director, the company is responsible for setting it up

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessStructureTrust

Inline content: Your business or businesslike activity is managed by a trustee for the benefit of others (the beneficiaries). A trustee is responsible for dealing with the trust, including lodging returns, distributing income or losses to the beneficiaries, and is also responsible for overseeing the payment of debts.

Help content:

Trusts

Using a trust structure means that a business or businesslike activity (such as charitable, leasing or one-off transactions) is held and controlled by a trustee for the benefit of others (the beneficiaries).

A trustee is responsible for everything in the trust, including income and losses, and must report income in a trust income tax return.

Tax File Number (TFN): A trust needs its own TFN. The good news is you don't need to register. The ATO will automatically issue a trust with a TFN (if one doesn't already exist).

A trustee can be an incorporated company or a person.

Key facts for trusts

  • Cost - higher cost to set up
  • Setting up process - complicated
  • Responsibility for business decisions - trustee
  • Responsibility for debts - trustee
  • Report business income - on trust income tax return
  • The money you get from the business - as a beneficiary, you report any distributions on your individual tax return (if you're the trustee and there is net trust income, you're taxed at the highest marginal tax rate)
  • Tax rate - depending on the trust deed, the trust may need to pay at the company tax rate. If all income is distributed to adult beneficiaries who are Australian residents, the trust doesn't need to pay tax.
  • Business Tax file number (TFN) needed - yes
  • Additional administration - the trustee is responsible for formal deed and yearly administrative tasks
  • Additional reporting - yes, the trustee is responsible for annual company tax return and other requirements
  • Separate bank account - yes

Find out more about trusts on the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) website.

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessStructureSoleTraderTip

Inline content: Sole traders use their individual Tax File Number (TFN) for both their personal and business dealings with the Australian Taxation Office (ATO). If you don't have one, you should also apply for an individual TFN.

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessStructureCompanyTip

Inline content: The cost of registering a company depends on the type of company. The most common company structure, a proprietary limited company, costs $479 to register.

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessStructureAbn

Help content:

Australian Business Number (ABN)

If you run a business in Australia, you should have an ABN.

An ABN is a unique 11 digit number that identifies your business to the public, the Australian Taxation Office (ATO), and other government agencies. Applying for an ABN is free.

Why should I have an ABN?

Having an ABN means you can:

  • confirm your business identity to others when ordering and invoicing
  • avoid having business clients withhold 46.5% of any payments they make to you
  • register a business name
  • register an Australian domain name.

You also need to have an ABN if you register for Goods and Services Tax (GST)

Find out more

Read about applying for an ABN on the Australian Business Register website.

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessStructureBusinessTfn

Help content:

Business Tax File Number (TFN)

All partnerships, companies and trusts need a business TFN to deal with the Australian Taxation Office (ATO), for example to report business income and deductions.

The good news is you don't need to register. The ATO will automatically issue a partnership, company or trust with a TFN (if one doesn't already exist).

Sole traders can use their individual TFN for both their personal and business matters.

Find out more about TFNs for businesses on the ATO website.

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessStructureCompanyRegistration

Help content:

Company registration

All companies need to be registered if they're operating in Australia. The cost of registering a company depends on the type of company. The most common company structure, a proprietary limited company, costs $479 to register.

Before you register

Before you register, you'll need to prepare and do things like:

  • decide whether your company will be private (proprietary) or public
  • decide on the officeholders (directors and secretaries)
  • choose a company name (that is, the company's legal name)
  • understand your legal obligations as a director and shareholder.

To find out more, read starting a company on the Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC) website.

When you've registered

When registered, the company will:

  • get an Australian Company Number (ACN) and a Certificate of Registration
  • appear in the company register
  • be required to complete an annual review and pay an annual review fee each year (see annual statements on the ASIC website).

To find out what company information is available to the public in the company register, go to companies and organisations - search ASIC's Registers.

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessStructurePartnershipTip

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessNameRegisterYes

Inline content: You need to register your business name if it's different to your own name.

For example, if your name is Sally Chang, and you want to use the name Accurate Accountants as your business name, you need to register it.

Help content:

Registering your business name

Registering a business name means you're registering it nationally with the Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC). This means you can use it in any state or territory. It costs $35 to register a business name for 1 year, and $82 for 3 years.

Before you register a business name, make sure it creates the right image for your business, and helps your business to stand out from your competitors.

Find out how you can:

  • choose an effective business name
  • check if a business name is available
  • register a business name.

Protecting your business name

It's important to know that registering your business name does not give you exclusive rights to it! Other businesses won't be able to register your name, but they can still use it to name a product or service.

If you trade mark your business name, it will be protected in all Australian states and territories for an initial period of 10 years. You can apply for a trade mark on the IP Australia website.

Websites and business names

Website addresses are called domain names, for example, sarahsflowers.com.au or josephsmitharchitects.com.au.

If you're keen to have domain name that reflects your business name, it's a good idea to register a domain name, even if you're not quite ready to set up your website. Generally prices range from $10 to $100 AUD for a year's registration.

Read our register a website name page for more information.

Having more than one business name

Did you know that you can register multiple business names? For example, if you run three businesses, you can register a different business name for each of them.

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessNameRegisterTip

Inline content:

Registering a business name costs $35 for 1 year, and $82 for 3 years.

Registering a business name doesn't stop other businesses from using it for their products or services! Find out how you can protect your business name.

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessNameRegisterNo

Inline content: You don't need to register your business name if you're using the names of all the partners.

For example, if the combined name of all the partners is Stephen White & George Black, and you want to use it (or a shortened form such as S. White & G. Black) as your business name, you don't need to register it.

Help content:

If you don?t need to register your business name

While you don't need to register your business name, it's a good idea to think about whether your business needs a website or social media accounts, and how you can connect your online presence to your business name.

Websites and business names

Website addresses are called domain names, for example, sarahanderson.com.au or josephsmith.com.au.

If you're keen to have a domain name that reflects your business, it's a good idea to register a domain name, even if you're not quite ready to set up your website. Generally prices range from $10 to $100 AUD for a year's registration.

Read our register a website name page for more information.

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessStructureOneOwnerTip

Inline content: If you want to own the business on your own, you can choose a sole trader or a company structure, but not a partnership.

Content key: HelpMeDecideEmployeesFringeBenefits

Help content:

Fringe benefits

Fringe benefits are non-cash benefits that you provide to employees or to their family members.

Common fringe benefits include:

  • a work car for private use
  • car parking within 1 km of a commercial car park
  • free or discounted loans, goods, or services
  • reimbursement of non-business expenses
  • free or low-rent housing
  • a living-away-from-home allowance
  • food or drink, such as meals and cocktail parties
  • entertainment, such as sporting events and movie tickets.

Content key: HelpMeDecideEmployeesPayg

Help content:

Pay As You Go (PAYG) withholding

When you have employees (this includes company directors), you must withhold tax amounts from the wage or salary payments you make to employees, and forward these tax amounts to the Australian Taxation Office (ATO).

PAYG withholding isn't a tax on your business - it refers to the amounts you're required to withhold from payments to your employees.

What do I need to do?

Being registered for PAYG withholding means you:

  • work out how much you need to withhold
  • report PAYG withholding amounts to the ATO
  • pay PAYG withholding amounts to the ATO when they're due
  • include PAYG withholding amounts in your employees' pay slips and payment summaries.

Find out more

Read about PAYG withholding on the ATO website.

Content key: HelpMeDecideEmployeesFbt

Help content:

Fringe Benefits Tax (FBT)

As an employer, if you give certain fringe benefits to your employees, you must pay tax on the value of the fringe benefits.

What do I need to do?

Being registered for FBT means you:

  • work out how much FBT you have to pay
  • keep records related to the fringe benefits you provide
  • report fringe benefits on your employees' payment summaries
  • report fringe benefits to the Australian Taxation Office (ATO)
  • pay the FBT to the ATO.

Find out more

Read about FBT on the ATO website.

Content key: HelpMeDecideActivityGst

Help content:

Goods and Services Tax (GST)

You should register for GST if you:

  • expect to have a turnover of $75,000 or more in your first year
  • charge passengers for taxi services (including ride sharing)
  • charge passengers for limousine travel.

Don't expect to exceed $75,000? You can still volunteer to register for GST.

Did you know? Registering for GST also allows you to claim credits for any GST included in the price of purchases for your business.

What is GST?

GST is a tax of 10% on most goods and services sold in Australia. The money raised by GST is given to the state and territory governments, to provide public services to their communities.

Businesses collect GST on the goods and services they sell and forward it to the Australian Taxation Office (ATO).

What do I need to do?

If you're registered for GST, you'll need to:

  • work out which goods and services you sell are subject to GST
  • include GST in the prices you charge for those goods and services
  • give tax invoices to your customers that include the GST amount
  • account for GST in your books
  • put aside the GST you've collected
  • get tax invoices and claim GST credits for goods and services you buy for your own business
  • lodge activity statements or annual returns to the ATO
  • pay GST to the ATO (or get a GST refund).

Find out more

Visit the ATO website to:

  • find out what GST turnover means
  • check if you need to register for GST, if you're not sure
  • see how GST applies to ride sharing services
  • find out more about claiming GST credits.

Content key: HelpMeDecideEmployeesYesNo

Help content:

Employees

Generally, employees:

  • can be casual, permanent, part-time or full-time
  • are supervised by an employer
  • are required to carry out their work in a particular way
  • earn a wage or salary
  • are entitled to paid leave
  • work set hours on a permanent, fixed term or casual basis
  • can't subcontract work tasks given to them.

Don't confuse employees with contractors! Contractors set up businesses to hire out their services to other businesses, and can negotiate their own working arrangements.

If you're not sure whether you'll be taking on an employee or a contractor, try our Independent contractors decision tool.

Content key: HelpMeDecideActivityTurnover

Help content:

Have business turnover of $75,000 or more in your first year

When we say business turnover, we mean your gross business income (not your profit).

Content key: HelpMeDecideActivityTaxiServices

Help content:

Run a taxi business

This includes:

  • drivers providing ride sharing services
  • taxi drivers who own their taxi
  • taxi drivers who lease or rent a taxi.

This doesn?t include car hire services where you don?t provide a driver.

Content key: HelpMeDecideActivityLimousine

Help content:

Run a limousine hire business

This doesn?t include limousine hire services where you don?t provide a driver.

Content key: HelpMeDecideActivityNoGst

Help content:

Goods and Services Tax (GST)

You don't have to register for GST if you:

  • don't expect to have a turnover of $75,000 or more in your first year
  • don't charge passengers for taxi services
  • don't charge passengers for limousine travel.

What is GST?

GST is a tax of 10% on most goods and services sold in Australia. The money raised by GST is given to the state and territory governments, to provide public services to their communities.

Businesses collect GST on the goods and services they sell and forward it to the Australian Taxation Office (ATO).

What do I need to do?

If you're registered for GST, you'll need to:

  • work out which goods and services you sell are subject to GST
  • include GST in the prices you charge for those goods and services
  • give tax invoices to your customers that include the GST amount
  • account for GST in your books
  • put aside the GST you've collected
  • get tax invoices and claim GST credits for goods and services you buy for your own business
  • lodge activity statements or annual returns to the ATO
  • pay GST to the ATO (or get a GST refund).

Find out more

Read about how GST works on the ATO website.

Content key: HelpMeDecideActivityWine

Help content:

Wholesale or produce wine in Australia

When we say wines, we mean grape wine, grape wine products such as vermouth, fruit or vegetable wines, cider, perry, mead, or sake, that has more than 1.15% ethyl alcohol.

Content key: HelpMeDecideActivityFuel

Help content:

Use taxable fuels in factories, equipment, machinery or heavy vehicles

When we say heavy vehicles, we mean any vehicle with a GVM (gross vehicle mass) greater than 4.5 tonnes.

Taxable fuels include:

  • liquid fuels:
    • petrol
    • diesel
    • kerosene
    • mineral turpentine
    • white spirit
    • toluene
    • heating oil
    • some solvents
  • gaseous fuels:
    • liquefied petroleum gas (LPG)
    • liquefied natural gas (LNG)
    • compressed natural gas (CNG)

Content key: HelpMeDecideActivityWet

Help content:

Wine Equalisation Tax (WET)

You must register for WET if:

  • you're registered for GST and
  • you import or produce wine or sell wines wholesale, where the wines are subject to WET.

What is WET?

WET is a tax on the consumption of wine in Australia, and it's usually applied to the last wholesale sale - this is usually the sale to the retailer. You collect the tax to give to the Australian Taxation Office (ATO).

What do I need to do?

Being registered for WET means you:

  • work out which sales are subject to WET
  • issue tax invoices that show the WET amount
  • claim any WET credits you may be entitled to (including rebates or refunds)
  • account for WET amounts in your books
  • put aside WET you've collected
  • report WET to the ATO.

Find out more

Read about WET on the ATO website.

Content key: HelpMeDecideActivityLuxuryCars

Help content:

Import or sell luxury cars

Luxury cars are cars that are worth more than $64,132 (more than $75,526 for fuel-efficient vehicles).

Content key: HelpMeDecideActivityLct

Help content:

Luxury Car Tax (LCT)

LCT is a tax on the supply and import of cars that exceed the LCT threshold.

You must register for LCT if:

  • you're registered for GST, and
  • you sell or import luxury cars.

What do I need to do?

Being registered for LCT means you:

  • work out the LCT payable and include it in the import or sale
  • account for LCT sales and imports in your books
  • report and pay LCT to the Australian Taxation Office (ATO).

Find out more

Read about LCT on the ATO website.

Content key: HelpMeDecideActivityFtc

Help content:

Fuel Tax Credits (FTC)

If you register for FTC, you can claim credits for the fuel tax you pay on these fuels. The amount of fuel tax credits you can claim depends on the type of fuel you use and what business activity you use it in.

What do I need to do?

Being registered for FTC means you:

  • work out how much you can claim on eligible fuels
  • keep records of the type and quantity of fuels, when you bought the fuels and how you used them in your business.

Am I eligible?

To be eligible, you must:

  • be registered for GST
  • use eligible fuels.

Try the FTC eligibility tool on the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) website.

Find out more

Read about FTC on the ATO website.

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessStructureTwoOrMoreTip

Inline content: If your business will have two or more owners, you can choose a partnership or company structure - but not a sole trader.

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessStructureHowManyOwners

Help content:

How many owners will your business have?

The number of owners may limit which business structure you can choose:

  • A sole trader can only have a single owner.
  • A company can have one or more owners.
  • A partnership can have two or more owners.
  • A trust can have one or more trustees and one or more beneficiaries.

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessStructureHoldControlAsset

Help content:

Will you hold and control an asset for the benefit of others?

A trust is where an individual or business manages an asset (estate, property or business) on behalf of beneficiaries (people who receive profits from the asset). A trust is treated like an entity for tax purposes.

Tax File Number (TFN): A trust needs its own TFN. The good news is you don't need to register. The ATO will automatically issue a trust with a TFN (if one doesn't already exist).

Trusts are expensive and complex to set up and you?ll need time and the right skills to do it properly. It's best to see a qualified, licensed professional to help you understand what?s involved.

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessStructureHoldControlAssetTip

Inline content: If you answered 'Yes' it means you (individual or business) will manage an asset (estate, property or business) that you want to protect on behalf of beneficiaries (people who receive profits from the asset).

Trusts are complex to set up and you'll need time and the right skills to do it properly. It's best to see a qualified, licensed professional to help you understand what's involved.

Content key: BusinessAssociateRuleSMF

Inline content:

Add business associates for your Self-Managed Superannuation Fund (SMSF) using the buttons below.

If your fund has more than one member:

AND has individual trustees:

  • You may have up to but no more than four members
  • Each member must be a trustee
  • All members cannot be employees of other members unless they are related.

OR has a corporate trustee:

  • Each member must be a director of the corporate trustee
  • All members cannot be employees of other members unless they are related.

If your fund has only one member:

AND has individual trustees:

  • The member must be a trustee and one more trustee must be appointed
  • The member cannot be an employee of the other trustee unless they are related.

OR has a corporate trustee:

  • The member must be a director of the corporate trustee
  • Another director of the corporate trustee may be appointed but there cannot be more than two
  • If there are two directors, the member cannot be an employee of the other director unless they are related.

For more information on how Self-Managed Superannuation Funds (SMSFs) are set up visit the Australian Taxation Office website or speak to a qualified professional.

Help content:

Business associates for an SMSF

Corporate trustees with one member

All directors should select both ?Director of the Corporate Trustee? and ?Member of Self-Managed Superannuation Fund?.

Individual trustees with one member

The member should select both ?Trustee? and ?Member of a Self-Managed Superannuation Fund?. The other appointed trustee should select ?Trustee? only.

Individual trustees with more than one member

Individual trustees should select both ?Trustee? and ?Member of a Self-Managed Superannuation Fund?.

Corporate trustees with more than one member

All directors should select both ?Director of the Corporate Trustee? and ?Member of Self-Managed Superannuation Fund?.

Content key: CompanyDetailsContactDetailsIsCompanyOccupyPremises

Inline content: If not, your company must have written consent from the lessee or owner to use the address as the registered office.

Help content:

Will the company occupy the address?

When we say 'occupy the address', we mean lease or own the property at the address.

If your company won't occupy the address, your company will need written consent from the lessee or owner.

For example, if your company's registered office is:

  • a property for which your company is on the rental lease ? your company will occupy the address (even if your company won't use the property)
  • the residential home of a company director (a home which they own, not the company) ? your company won't occupy the address and will need written consent
  • the office of your company's accountant, tax agent or solicitor ? your company won't occupy the address and will need written consent.

Find out more about company addresses on the Australian Securities & Investments Commission website.

  3. enter the island name in Address line 3
  4. enter your postcode (6798 for Christmas Island, 6799 for Cocos Island and 2899 for Norfolk Island).

Content key: AllFieldsRequiredUnlessOptional

Content key: ConfirmationNotes

Inline content: Here's your chance to check that you've entered all the details correctly. If you find an error on this page, select the Edit button next to a section to return to that part of the form.

Help content:

Confirmation

What if my details change?

If your details change, you'll have 28 days from when you were aware of the change to:

  • update your tax registration details
  • update your business name details
  • make changes to your company.

Are there penalties for incorrect information?

There are penalties if the information entered is false or misleading. However, if you let the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) or the Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC) know as soon as you're aware of the error or if you update them before they contact you, you may not receive a penalty.

See the Making a false or misleading statement page on the Australian Business Register (ABR) website.

For business name or company details, see the Late fees section of the ASIC website.

What happens to my form after I submit?

Once you accept the declaration and submit the form, your application will be sent to the ABR and/or ASIC to be processed.

Can I access my form details after I submit?

No, once you submit the form, you'll no longer have access to the details in your form.

How can I check on the progress of my application?

The status of your application can be checked on the Application summary page. Here you'll see whether your application was successful. You'll also be able to see the reason if it was not successful.

Email address

name@emailprovider.com.

Your email address may be used to send you notices or correspondence.

Please check that your email address meets the following formatting rules:

  • between 5 and 200 characters
  • contain at least one dot (.) and only one @ symbol
  • contain no spaces
  • contain only valid characters. We will accept:
    • 0-9
    • A-Z
    • a-z
    • -
    • _
    • .
    • @

Content key: BusinessDetailsBusinessAddressContactDetailsPhone
