Phone number

Please enter a valid phone number. For example:

01 2345 6789 or for mobile 0123 456 789

For Australian numbers, please include the state or territory area code, but don't use brackets.

For island numbers, you can only enter numbers with the following formats:

  • Christmas island: 08 9164 1111
  • Cocos island: 08 9162 1111
  • Norfolk island: 0011 67232 1111

For international numbers, please drop the "+" sign and enter the country code, area code and number. For example:

61 2 3456 7890

Content key: TaxationLctDetailsAnnualLiabilityTaxationLctDetailsAnnualLiability

Help content:

Estimate of LCT you will pay in the next 12 months

Give a rough estimate of the LCT amount you think you'll need to pay in the next 12 months. LCT is 33% of the value of a luxury car above the LCT threshold.

As a guide, here's how to work out the LCT amount if you sell or import a car:

(LCT value ? LCT threshold) x 10/11 x 33%

The LCT value generally includes:

  • any GST, and
  • the value of any parts, accessories or attachments you supplied or imported at the same time as the car.

Visit the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) website to find out more about:

  • working out the LCT on an import
  • working out the LCT on a sale.

Why do I need to provide an estimate?

The ATO uses your estimate to prefill the LCT amount payable on your first BAS (business activity statement) form before they send it to you.

The BAS form is for you to report and pay LCT, along with any other tax registrations.

If you need to, you can change the LCT amount on the BAS form based on your actual sales or imports for that period.

Content key: TaxationFctDetailsFuelType

Help content:

Types of fuel

The Australian Taxation Office (ATO) asks this question to help them understand what types of fuels are being used in businesses.

You can claim FTC on taxable fuels you use in your business, like petrol and diesel.

Other taxable fuels you can claim FTC on include:

  • liquid fuels - such as kerosene, mineral turpentine, white spirit, toluene, heating oil, and some solvents
  • gaseous fuels - liquefied petroleum gas (LPG), liquefied natural gas (LNG) and compressed natural gas (CNG).

Find out more about eligible fuels for FTC on the ATO website.

Content key: CompanyDetailsAllocateSharesNumberOfShares

Help content:

Number of shares

Enter the number of shares the shareholder has agreed in writing to take up.

A company can issue as many shares as it likes, but it must issue at least 1 share to at least 1 shareholder. The split of shares usually shows the percentage split in ownership.

For example, if a company is owned by 2 equal shareholders, they could take up:

  • 50 shares each (representing the 50/50 split) or
  • just 1 share each.

If a company is owned by 1 majority shareholder and 2 other shareholders, the company could issue 100 shares:

  • 60 shares for the majority shareholder, and
  • 20 shares each to the other 2 shareholders.

Important! If you?re not sure how many company shares to issue, consider getting advice from a business adviser or other professional.

Content key: CompanyDetailsAllocateSharesAmountPaidPerShare

Help content:

Amount paid per share

Enter the amount the shareholder has paid per share. This may be 100% of the share price that they agreed to in writing, or part of it.

The amount paid per share depends on the type of shares and whether there were any arrangements made for their purchase.

The amount paid per share plus the amount unpaid per share must equal the agreed share price per share.

For example:

  • Fully paid shares (where shares are paid up front)
    Agreed share price = $10
    Amount paid per share = $10
    Amount unpaid per share = $0
  • Partially paid shares (where only part payment is required up front)
    Agreed share price = $10
    Amount paid per share = $7
    Amount unpaid per share = $3

The amounts paid for company shares form part of the company's capital and are usually paid into the company's bank account.

Did you know? Shares are usually priced between 1 cent and $1 when they?re first issued, but the company can issue them at any price greater than 0.

Important! If you?re not sure about the price of your shares, consider getting advice from a business adviser or other professional.

Content key: CompanyDetailsAllocateSharesAmountUnpaidPerShare

Help content:

Amount unpaid per share

Enter the amount unpaid per share.

If the shares have been fully paid, enter 0.

Remember: the amount paid per share plus the amount unpaid per share must equal the agreed share price per share.

Content key: CompanyDetailsPersonAssociatePlaceOfBirth

Inline content: Enter the city or town.

Help content:

Place of birth

Please only enter the city or town of birth and check it:

  • is spelled out in full (e.g. Mount Gambier not Mt Gambier)
  • doesn?t include the hospital or suburb (e.g. Perth not Osborne Park Hospital, Stirling)
  • uses sentence case (e.g. Swan Hill not Swan hill).

Important: This field helps to prove the identity of this person. If the birth details are entered incorrectly, your registration may be rejected by the Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC).

Still not sure?

Entering the Place of birth on behalf of someone else? If you want to make sure you?ve entered the place name correctly, you can check it on the Australia Place Name Search on the Geoscience Australia website.

Content key: EntitlementCompanyTypeHUNT

Inline content: For companies that act as a body corporate to manage a block of flats or similar properties.

Help content:

Home unit company

A home unit company is made up of directors and/or shareholders who own or live in a block of flats (or similar building). The company acts as a body corporate to manage the property.

Home unit companies are considered 'special purpose' companies. This means they are set up for a specific purpose only and qualify for lower annual review fees.

The constitutions of all special purpose companies must meet Regulation 3 of the Corporations (Review Fees) Regulations 2003.

Find out more

  • Read about special purpose companies on the Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC) website.
  • View Regulation 3 of the Corporations (Review Fees) Regulations 2003 on the Legislation Register website.
If you're not sure whether the company's constitution meets the requirements, consider getting independent legal advice.

Content key: EntitlementCompanyTypePNPC

Inline content: For charities and other charitable organisations.

Help content:

Not-for-profit company

A not-for-profit company must be for charitable purposes only. The company's constitution must:

  • direct the company to use its income to promote these charitable purposes
  • not allow the company to make distributions to its shareholders or pay fees to its directors
  • require its directors to approve any payments the company makes to directors or shareholders
  • meet Regulation 3 of the Corporations (Review Fees) Regulations 2003.

Not-for-profit companies are considered 'special purpose' companies. This means they are set up for a specific purpose only and qualify for lower annual review fees.

Find out more

  • Read about special purpose companies on the Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC) website.
  • View Regulation 3 of the Corporations (Review Fees) Regulations 2003 on the Legislation Register website.

If you're not sure whether the company's constitution meets the requirements, consider getting independent legal advice.

Content key: EntitlementCompanyTypePROP

Inline content: This is the most commonly used company structure in Australia. In proprietary limited companies, the personal liability of shareholders is limited to the amount unpaid (if any) on their shares.

Help content:

Proprietary limited company

'Proprietary limited' is the most common company structure used by businesses in Australia.

In proprietary limited companies, the liability of shareholders is limited to any unpaid amount on their shares. The personal assets and property of shareholders can't be used to pay for any company debts.

For example:

  • One $20 share is issued to a shareholder.
  • The shareholder pays $15, leaving $5 unpaid.
  • If the company is wound up, the shareholder maybe be liable to contribute the unpaid $5 to help pay the debts of the company, but nothing more.

What about proprietary unlimited companies?

Generally, unlimited companies are only used if there is a requirement for shareholders to have unlimited liability. Most businesses don't have this requirement.

You can't apply to register unlimited companies here. You'll need to download the company registration form on the Australian Securities & Investments Commission website.

Content key: EntitlementCompanyTypePSTC

Inline content: For trustees of regulated super funds.

Help content:

Superannuation trustee company

A superannuation trustee company acts as a trustee of a regulated superannuation fund.

The company's constitution must:

  • not allow the company to distribute income or property to its shareholders
  • meet Regulation 3 of the Corporations (Review Fees) Regulations 2003.

Superannuation trustee companies are considered 'special purpose' companies. This means they are set up for a specific purpose only and qualify for lower annual review fees.

Find out more

  • Read about special purpose companies on the Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC) website.
  • For the definition of 'regulated superannuation fund', see s19 Superannuation Industry (Supervision) Act 1993 on the Legislation Register website.
  • View Regulation 3 of the Corporations (Review Fees) Regulations 2003 on the Legislation Register website.

If you're not sure whether the company's constitution meets the requirements, consider getting independent legal advice.

Content key: EntitlementCompanyTypeAPUB

Inline content: Public companies must have at least three directors, one secretary and one shareholder. They can raise funds by selling shares to the public.

Help content:

Public company

A public company can:

  • have an unlimited number of non-employee shareholders
  • raise funds by selling shares to the general public
  • be listed on a financial or share market (if it meets requirements).

A public company must have at least:

  • one shareholder (or member)
  • one secretary, and
  • three directors.

Individuals can hold more than one role. For example, you can be both a shareholder, a secretary, and a director.

If you're not sure whether a private or public company structure best suits your business, consider getting advice from an independent professional.

Content key: DashboardDescription

Inline content:

Welcome to the application summary! Here you can:

  • resume unfinished applications
  • track the status of applications
  • search for specific applications.

What's next?

Visit business.gov.au for information on:

  • starting a business
  • government grants and assistance
  • events and training near you.

Help content:

Application summary

Where to now?

There are a number of registrations you may still need before you can start trading. State and territories have various licences and permits that are often required prior to starting your business or company.

See the Australian Business Licence and Information Service (ABLIS) to find the licences and permits you need to run your business.

How do I change my form details?

If you?ve made a mistake in your company or business name, you must notify ASIC within 24 hours after the registration. Generally if there?s a typing error (e.g. letters are mixed up or missing), ASIC will correct it.

If your details have changed, you have 28 days from when you were aware of the change to:

  • update your tax registration details
  • update your business name details
  • make changes to your company.

Are there penalties for incorrect information?

There are penalties if the information entered is false or misleading. However, if you let the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) or Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC) know as soon as you're aware of the error or if you update them before they contact you, you may not receive a penalty.

See the Making a false or misleading statement page on the Australian Business Register (ABR) website.

For business name or company details, see the Late fees section of the ASIC website.

Where can I register for a business TFN?

If you're registering an ABN for a partnership, company or trust, you'll need a business TFN.

The good news is you don't need to register. The ATO will automatically issue a partnership, company or trust with a TFN (if one doesn't already exist).

Where do I renew my business name?

See the different options available for renewing your business name on the ASIC website.

Content key: EntitlementCompanyPublicSubclassLegend

Inline content: Sub-class of company

Content key: CompanyNameNewName

Help content:

Create a new name

If you choose to create a new name, we'll first check it's available.

A name is not available if it's:

  • been registered or reserved by another company
  • the same as a registered business name (unless someone associated with your company owns it)
  • considered unacceptable under the Corporations Act 2001 (for example, if it is likely to cause offence to the public, or suggest a connection with a government authority).
Important!
  • We don't check whether your name has been trade marked.

    Before you register your company name here, make sure you're not infringing on someone else's copyright. Go to the IP Australia website to find out about searching for trade marks

  • Registering a company name stops others from using it as a company name, but it doesn't stop others from using it for their website or for their product.

    If you want to get exclusive use of your company name, apply for a trade mark on the IP Australia website. If you want to use it as part of your web address, see our register a domain name page.

Visit the Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC) website to find out more about:

  • company name availability
  • choosing company names - see step 2 of starting a company.

Reserving company names

Have a proposed company name but not ready to submit your company registration application? If you want to make sure no one else takes your name, you can apply to reserve it for up to 2 months.

Go to the ASIC website to download the application form for reservation of a name and for information about the reservation application fees.

Content key: CompanyNameReservedName

Help content:

Use a reserved name

Choose this option if you've already reserved your company name with the Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC) and have received a Reservation Notice.

When you enter the reservation details, make sure:

  • your reservation number has 9 digits (you can find this number on your Reservation Notice)
  • the company name (including the legal element) is exactly the same as the name in your reservation
  • your reservation is still current (company names are reserved for 2 months).

If you have any questions about your company name reservation, contact ASIC.

Content key: BusinessStructureSoleTraderDescription

Inline content:

The sole trader structure is the most common business structure in Australia. It?s the cheapest and easiest to set up, and generally suits people who:

  • don?t want to do extra tax reporting
  • want to use any business losses to offset their other personal income
  • want to claim business expenses to reduce their personal income
  • have a low risk of going into significant business debt

Content key: BusinessStructureSoleTraderTip

Inline content:

This is only a guide! Talk to a professional if you're not sure about what business structure you'd prefer.

Content key: BusinessStructureSoleTraderPros

Inline content:

  • Cheapest structure
  • Simple to set up and run
  • No additional reporting
  • All profits belong to you
  • You have complete control
  • Easy to change to a different structure
  • Easy to sell or close your business

Content key: BusinessStructureSoleTraderCons

Inline content:

  • You risk losing your personal assets if your business goes into debt
  • You?re responsible for all taxes and losses
  • You?re responsible for organising your own superannuation
  • You?re not an employee of your business, so you don?t get paid annual or sick leave

Content key: BusinessStructurePartnershipDescription

Inline content:

A partnership is where two or more people own a business together and share the income and losses. It?s relatively inexpensive and easy to set up and operate, and generally suits people who:

  • want to minimise their reporting requirements
  • want to use any business losses to offset their other personal income
  • want to claim business expenses to reduce their personal income
  • have low risk of going into significant business debt

Content key: BusinessStructurePartnershipPros

Inline content:

  • Relatively cheap to set up
  • Simple to set up and run
  • Minimal reporting requirements
  • Your share of the profits belong to you

Content key: BusinessStructurePartnershipCons

Inline content:

  • You risk losing your personal assets if your business goes into debt, even if your partner was responsible
  • You?re responsible for organising your own superannuation
  • You?re not an employee of your business, so you don?t get paid annual or sick leave
  • Sharing business decisions can sometimes result in disputes

Based on your selection, you think the company structure might suit your business needs.

Content key: BusinessStructureCompanyTip

Inline content:

This is only a guide! Talk to a professional if you?re not sure about what business structure you?d prefer.

Content key: BusinessStructureCompanyDescription

Inline content:

A company structure means your business is a separate legal entity from you. This means that it can borrow money and be sued like a person.

A company has at least one owner (shareholder) and one director. You can be both.

Companies are expensive and complicated to set up, and generally suit people who:

  • expect their business income to be highly variable, and want the option to use losses to offset future profits
  • think their business has a high risk of going into debt, and want to limit the risk of losing personal assets such as their home (limit their liability).

Content key: BusinessStructureCompanyTipLegal

Inline content:

It?s important to know that companies must meet complex reporting and legal requirements.

Content key: BusinessStructureCompanyPros

Inline content:

  • Limited liability for owners
  • Limited liability for directors, if they meet their legal obligations
  • Ability to raise money by offering company shares
  • Profits can be invested back into the company or distributed to owners
  • Easy to sell or pass on to another owner
  • Can carry losses forward to offset future profits

Content key: BusinessStructureCompanyCons

Inline content:

  • Expensive and complicated to set up
  • Complex reporting and legal requirements
  • Can?t distribute losses
  • Limited or no control of company affairs

Content key: BusinessStructureTrustDescription

Inline content:

In a trust structure, a trustee holds your business for the benefit of others (the beneficiaries).

A trustee can be a person or a company, and is responsible for everything in the trust, including income and losses.

Trust structures are expensive and complicated to set up, and are generally used to protect the business assets for beneficiaries. The trustee decides how business profits should be distributed to the beneficiaries.

Content key: BusinessStructureTrustTipProfits

Inline content:

If profits are invested back into the business, the business must pay penalty tax rates on the profits.

  • Limited liability ? especially if the trustee is a company
  • Flexibility of asset and income distribution

Content key: BusinessStructureTrustCons

Inline content:

  • Expensive and complicated to set up
  • Difficult to end or change the trust once it?s been set up
  • Pay penalty rates on any profits invested back into the business

Content key: CompanyIsOfficeHoldersAddressResidential

Inline content:

Company directors and secretaries must provide their usual residential address. They can use a different Australian address if they have approval from the Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC).

Help content:

Residential addresses of officeholders

Company officeholders (directors and secretaries) must provide their usual residential address.

'Usual' residential address means the address that they normally live at for most of the time.

They can apply for approval to use a different Australian address in place of their usual residential address if:

  • the Australian Electoral Commission has given them a silent enrolment status, or
  • their name isn't on the Australian electoral roll and having their residential address on the public company register would risk the personal safety of the officeholder or the officeholder's family.
The address used in place of the usual residential address must be in Australia and be one where documents can be served on the officeholder. The residential requirements for company officeholders still apply.

Visit the Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC) website for information about the application fee and how to apply for suppression of residential address.

Read the summary of ASIC's privacy policy to find out how ASIC handles personal information.

Special purpose company declaration

You've chosen to set up a special purpose company (a home unit company, a not for profit company, or a superannuation trustee company).

Before you can continue with the form, you need to declare that the company (and the company's constitution) meets the requirements of a special purpose company.

You can view the requirements in Regulation 3 of the Corporations (Review Fees) Regulations 2003 on the Federal Register of Legislation website.

Content key: CompanyConstitutionUpload

Inline content: Upload a copy of the company's constitution. You can upload a Word, PDF or image file.

Help content:

Company constitution

These types of companies must have a written constitution:

  • Public companies limited by guarantee who want to omit the word 'Limited' from their company name (under section 150 of the Corporations Act 2001)
  • 'No liability' public companies
  • 'Special purpose' companies

What is a constitution?

A constitution outlines the rules for how a company is managed or run (such as how to appoint directors and how to run and call shareholder meetings).

Find out more about constitutions on the Australian Securities & Investments Commission website.

Content key: EntitlementIsSharesIssuedForNonCashConsiderationLegend

Inline content: Is the company issuing shares for anything other than cash?

Content key: CompanyDetailsOfficeAddressHelpText

Inline content: Must be a street address in Australia. Official documents and notices related to your company registration will be sent to this address.

Help content:

Registered office address

All companies must have a registered office in Australia to receive official documents and notices. The registered office is where government agencies, members of the public and others will direct their queries about company matters (and serve legal documents).

The company's registered office can be wherever you like, as long as it's a street address in Australia (not a post box).

A company's registered office could be:

  • the office of the company's accountant
  • the street address of the company's retail store
  • the home address of a company director.

The registered office address will be available to the public in the company register on the Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC) website.

Find out more about company addresses on the ASIC website.

Content key: EntitlementCompanyClassHelpText

Content key: EntitlementCompanyTypeLMSH

Inline content: In a company limited by shares, the personal liability of shareholders is limited to the amount unpaid (if any) on their shares.

Help content:

Limited by shares

'Limited by shares' is the most common class used by public companies in Australia.

When a company is 'limited by shares', the liability of shareholders is limited to any unpaid amount on their shares. The personal assets and property of shareholders can't be used to pay for any company debts.

For example:

  • One $20 share is issued to a shareholder.
  • The shareholder pays $15, leaving $5 unpaid.
  • If the company is wound up, the shareholder maybe be liable to contribute the unpaid $5 to help pay the debts of the company, but nothing more.

What about unlimited companies?

Generally, unlimited companies are only used if there is a requirement for shareholders to have unlimited liability. Most businesses don't have this requirement.

You can't apply to register unlimited companies here. You'll need to download the company registration form on the Australian Securities & Investments Commission website.

Content key: EntitlementCompanyTypeLMGT

Inline content: Generally used by not-for-profit or charitable organisations.

Help content:

Limited by guarantee

This class is generally for not-for-profit or charitable organisations.

When individuals become members of a 'limited by guarantee' company, they must agree to contribute an amount if the company is wound up.

This means the liability of the company?s members is limited to the amount they 'guarantee' to contribute.

Go to the Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC) website to find out more about:

  • companies limited by guarantee
  • registering not for profit or charitable organisations.

ACN or ARBN

ACN is Australian Company Number, a unique 9 digit number that identifies Australian companies. The Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC) issues ACNs to companies when they're successfully registered.

ARBN is Australian Registered Body Number. ASIC issues these to foreign companies and other bodies when they're successfully registered.

ACN or ARBN

ARBN is Australian Registered Body Number. ASIC issues these to foreign companies and other bodies when they're successfully registered.

Content key: CompanyDetailsAuthorisedContactDetailsLegend

Inline content:

You need to provide at least one authorised contact who can be contacted by the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) for queries or more information about this application.

If you nominate a tax agent, you should also provide their tax agent number.

Help content:

Authorised contact

You can choose to nominate one or more people to be authorised contacts for the company's business name, ABN and tax registrations.

For ABN or tax registration, an authorised contact can ask the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) to change details on behalf of the company. Find out more about ATO authorised contacts on the ATO website.

For business name registration, an authorised contact can complete transactions with the Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC) on behalf of the company. Find out more about Authorised lodgers for business names on the ASIC website.

An authorised contact can't add, remove or update authorised contacts unless they declare they've been given authority to do so by the company.

Any individual can be an authorised contact, as long as they meet POI (proof of identity) requirements. Authorised contacts can include:

  • registered tax agents
  • registered BAS agents
  • lawyers
  • guardians
  • family members or friends.

Content key: CheckAbnEntitledBeforeContinue

Inline content:

Answer the following questions to help us help work out if you're entitled to an ABN. Details on ABN entitlement can be found on the Australian Business Register (ABR) website.

Content key: CompanyDetailsPersonAssociateOfficeDirector

Help content:

Directors

Directors are expected to:

  • guide and monitor the management of the company
  • always act in the company's best interests
  • ensure the company complies with its legal requirements.

To be a director, a person must:

  • have agreed in writing to be a company director
  • be at least 18 years of age.

A director's appointment is effective on the day the company is successfully registered.

For more about the duties of a company director, go to the Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC) website:

  • Your company and the law
  • Company officeholder duties

What personal information will be available to the public?

The name, date of birth and residential address of directors will be available to the public from the company register for a small fee.

Read the summary of ASIC's privacy policy to find out how ASIC handles personal information.

Who can't be a director?

Unless a court allows it, a person must not be a director if they:

  • are bankrupt
  • are in debt and have an agreement with creditors to manage the debt
  • have been convicted of offences such as fraud or offences under the Corporations Act 2001
  • have been disqualified from managing companies.

Find out more about bankruptcy and personal insolvency agreements while you're a director on the ASIC website.

Content key: CompanyDetailsPersonAssociateOfficeSecretary

Help content:

Secretaries

The day-to-day responsibilities of company secretaries may include:

  • letting ASIC know about changes to company details (such as changes to the registered office address, secretaries or directors)
  • lodging the company's financial reports
  • ensuring the company meets its legal requirements.

To be a secretary, a person must:

  • have agreed in writing to be a company secretary
  • be at least 18 years of age.

A secretary's appointment is effective on the day the company is successfully registered.

Find out more about the duties of officeholders (directors and secretaries) on the Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC) website.

What personal information will be available to the public?

The name, date of birth and residential address of company secretaries will be available to the public from the company register for a small fee.

Read the summary of ASIC's privacy policy to find out how ASIC handles personal information.

Who can't be a secretary?

Unless a court allows it, a person must not be a secretary if they:

  • are bankrupt
  • are in debt and have an agreement with creditors to manage the debt
  • have been convicted of offences such as fraud or offences under the Corporations Act 2001.

Content key: CompanyDetailsPersonAssociateOfficeShareholder

Help content:

Shareholders

Shareholders are also known as members. Together, they own the company by owning shares in the company.

Remember, however, that the company is a separate legal entity, so the company's assets belong to the company, not the shareholders.

Shareholders can be individuals or companies. Company law doesn't set out a minimum age requirement for shareholders, but a company can make rules about this if they like.

Find out more about the role of company members (shareholders) on the ASIC website.

What information will be available to the public?

The name and residential address of company members (shareholders) will be available to the public from the company register for a small fee.

Read the summary of ASIC's privacy policy to find out how ASIC handles personal information.

Content key: BusinessAssociateRuleAPTY+PRV

Inline content:

Please add all the business associates related to the company and include:

  • at least 1 director (with at least 1 who lives in Australia)
  • at least 1 shareholder (can be individuals or companies)
  • 1 public officer - this person will be contacted by the Australian Taxation Office if they have any queries about the company's tax affairs

If you're also applying for an Australian Business Number, you'll also need:

  • one or more secretaries (with at least 1 who lives in Australia).
Did you know? Individuals can hold more than one role. For example, you can set up a one person company and hold all of the roles listed above.

  • at least 3 directors (with at least 2 who lives in Australia)
  • at least 1 shareholder (can be individuals or companies)
  • at least 1 secretary (with at least 1 who lives in Australia)
  • 1 public officer - this person will be contacted by the Australian Taxation Office if they have any queries about the company's tax affairs.
Did you know? Individuals can hold more than one role. For example, you can be both a director, a secretary, a shareholder and a public officer.

Content key: CompanyDetailsPersonAssociateMember

Help content:

Members

Members of limited by guarantee companies don't pay (or agree to pay) for shares in the company. Instead, they agree to contribute an amount of money if the company is wound up.

Members can be individuals or organisations. Company law doesn't set out a minimum age requirement for members, but a company can make rules about this if they like.

Find out more about the role of company members on the ASIC website.

What information will be available to the public?

The name and residential address of company members will be available to the public from the company register for a small fee.

Content key: BusinessActivityIsIncomeTaxExempt

Help content:

Income tax exempt

Some types of companies and organisations may be exempt from income tax if they meet certain requirements. This means they don't need to pay income tax.

Companies and organisations that can be exempt from income tax include:

  • not-for-profit organisations such as community, sporting and health organisations
  • charities.

Before a charity can be exempt from income tax, it must:

  1. register with the Australian Charities and Not-for-profits Commission
  2. be endorsed by the Australian Taxation Office (ATO).

Companies and organisations that aren't charities can work out for themselves if they are exempt from income tax.

To find out more, go to the ATO website:

  • Types of income tax exempt organisations
  • Work out if your organisation is income tax exempt

Content key: BusinessActivityIsNonProfitOrganisation

Help content:

Not for profit

A company is not for profit if it:

  • uses any profit it makes to achieve its aims
  • doesn't pay dividends to its members
  • doesn't distribute its assets to members if it is wound up.

Not making any profit doesn't mean your company is a not-for-profit organisation.

Examples of not-for-profit organisations include:

  • religious institutions
  • cultural societies
  • professional associations
  • social clubs.

To find out more, go to the What type of not for profit is your organisation? page on the Australian Taxation Office website.

Content key: BusinessActivityIsGovernmentOwnedOrControlled

Inline content:

Please note: Government entities are entitled to an ABN but cannot apply using this service. If you would like to apply for an ABN as a government entity visit the Australian Business Register.

Help content:

Government control

A government controls a company or organisation if the government controls it through either:

  • majority ownership of voting shares
  • specific legislation, or
  • the right to appoint and remove most 'directors' of the controlling body of the company or organisation.

Because of this control, a government agency can influence the operations of a company or organisation.

A company or organisation isn't controlled by a government simply because it receives most of its funding from a government.

Governments at all levels (Commonwealth, state, territory and local) can control a company or organisation.

Content key: CompanyDetailsPersonAssociateOfficePublicOfficer

Help content:

Public officers

All public companies and limited partnerships must appoint a public officer.

Public officers must be an individual and at least 18 years of age.

Public officers are responsible for making sure the company complies with tax law, keeping tax records and submitting company tax returns.

The Australian Taxation Office (ATO) will contact public officers about tax matters related to the company.

Find out more about public officers on the ATO website.

Help content:

Members

Members of limited by guarantee companies don't pay (or agree to pay) for shares in the company. Instead, they agree to contribute an amount of money if the company is wound up.

Members can be individuals or organisations. Company law doesn't set out a minimum age requirement for members, but a company can make rules about this if they like.

Find out more about the role of company members on the Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC) website.

Content key: CompanyDetailsPersonAssociateOfficeOfficeBearerofaclubassociation

Office bearer of a club or association

Office bearers include:

  • presidents
  • treasurers
  • secretaries.

Content key: CompanyDetailsPersonAssociateOfficeTrustee

Help content:

Trustee

Trustees can be an individual or a company.

'Company' includes any of the following:

  • Australian private company
  • Australian public company
  • Other incorporated entity
  • Co-operative
  • Public trading trust

The trustee of a Self-Managed Superannuation Fund (SMSF) is responsible for controlling the fund and making investment decisions.

Content key: CompanyDetailsPersonAssociateOfficePartner

Help content:

Partner

Partners can be an individual, company, registered body or other incorporated body.

A partnership can't be a partner in another partnership, except in the case of limited partnerships.

Family and other partnerships

There is no legal paperwork required to form a family or other partnership. However, there are legal responsibilities that come with being a partner.

Each partner must report their share of the income on their own individual tax return, and pay tax at their individual income tax rate.

The partnership must also lodge a partnership income tax return every year with the Australian Taxation Office (ATO).

All partners share legal responsibility and any debt equally.

If your business employs staff, all partners are responsible for giving staff their minimum employee entitlements and conditions (such as superannuation and leave).

Limited partnerships

Partners in a limited partnership must register the partnership and comply with their relevant state or territory partnership legislation. This legislation is administered by the Consumer affairs or Fair trading office in each state and territory.

A limited partnership is considered a company for tax purposes and the partnership must lodge a company tax return with the Australian Taxation Office (ATO).

Not all partners share legal responsibility for the business. The limited partners only share responsibility and debt according to the limitations set out in the state or territory registration.

Find out more

More on the partnership business structure can be found on the business.gov.au website.

To find out if you're a partnership for tax purposes, visit the Australian Taxation Office website.

Content key: EntitlementEntitySubTypeDES

Inline content: Used to finalise the estate (e.g. a business or property) of a deceased owner.

Help content:

Deceased estate

A deceased estate is a type of trust where a business or property needs to be finalised following the death of the owner.

To be entitled to an Australian Business Number (ABN), a deceased estate must:

  • be carrying on a business or businesslike activity of a deceased person for the purposes of getting the affairs in order.
  • have a trustee (e.g. an executor named in a will or administrator appointed by a court).

An executor finalising a deceased estate may carry out activities that meet the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) requirements of 'carrying on a business?, which entitles the deceased estate to an ABN.

Important: This ABN application does not set up a deceased estate! A deceased estate must already exist to continue with your application.

Not already a deceased estate?

Trusts are complex to set up and you?ll need time and the right skills to do it properly.

It's best to see a qualified, licensed professional to help you understand what?s involved and the registrations you'll need.

Visit the ATO website for more information on deceased estates.

Once you?re ready, come back to our website to apply for an ABN

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessNameRegisterYesSoletrader

Inline content: You need to register your business name if it's different to your own name.

For example, if your name is Sally Chang, and you want to use the name Accurate Accountants as your business name, you need to register it.

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessNameRegisterYesCompany

Inline content: You need to register a business name if it's different to your company name (the legal name you give your company when you register it).

For example, if your company name is Dairy Exports Pty Ltd and you want to carry on a business under the name Fresh Dairy Products, you need to register Fresh Dairy Products as a business name.

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessNameRegisterYesPartnership

Inline content: You need to register your business name if it's different to the names of all the partners.

For example, if the names of the partners are Stephen White & George Black, and you want to use the name Black & White Accountants as your business name, you need to register it.

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessNameRegisterNoSoletrader

Inline content: You don't need to register your business name if you're using your own name.

For example, if your name is Joe Aaron Smith, and you want to use it (or a shortened form such as Joe Smith, J Smith, or J. A. Smith) as your business name, you don't need to register it.

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessNameRegisterNoCompany

Inline content: You don't need to register a business name if it's the same as your company name (the legal name you give your company when you register it).

For example, if your company name is Dairy Exports Pty Ltd and you want to carry on a business under this name, you don't need to register it as a business name.

Content key: HelpMeDecideBusinessNameRegisterNoPartnership

Inline content: You don't need to register your business name if you're using the names of all the partners.

For example, if the combined name of all the partners is Stephen White & George Black, and you want to use it (or a shortened form such as S. White & G. Black) as your business name, you don't need to register it.

Content key: CompanyNameLegalElement

Help content:

Legal element

All company names have a legal element to identify the company type.

Please note: Although you can choose your legal element (e.g. PTY LTD or PTY Limited), it does form part of your legal name. This means when you enter your name on official documents, you must include the legal element exactly as it is on your registration.

Proprietary limited companies

You can choose 'Proprietary Limited' or a shortened version of it, such as 'Pty Ltd'.

Public companies limited by shares or guarantee

You can choose 'Limited' or a shortened version of it, such as 'Ltd'.

You can choose to not have a legal element in the company name if the company meets certain requirements. To view the requirements, see Section 150 of the Corporations Act 2001 on the Legislation Register website.

No liability companies

You can choose 'No liability' or a shortened version of it, such as 'NL'.

    Content key: BusinessDetailsPOIIntro

    Inline content:

    The details below are used to confirm your identity.

    When you apply for an ABN and other tax registrations, your identity will be verified with the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) against your tax records. This allows us to confirm who you are and helps to prevent identity theft.

    If your details don't match the ATO's records, you'll be asked check your details and correct any errors.

    Please ensure your details with the ATO are up to date! To update your tax details visit the ATO website. @HelpLink

    Help content:

    Proof of Identity (POI)

    The Australian Business Register (ABR) will check the personal details you enter here against the existing details on the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) systems. If these details don't match, then your application may need to be assessed manually or your application may be unsuccessful.

    Please ensure your details with the ATO are up to date! To update your tax details visit the ATO website.

    Where can I find out more?

    Details on what information the ABR needs to prove your identity can be found on the ABR website.

    Content key: ChooseStructureSoleTraderDecisions

    Inline content: Business decisions are made by: You Directors

    Help content:

    Who makes business decisions?

    As a sole trader, you're responsible for all business decisions.

    In a company, the directors are responsible for business decisions and must make a record of certain decisions. If you?re the only director, you have full control.

    If there is more than one director, the way the company will be run (including how decisions are made) will follow certain rules. These rules are usually set out in the company's constitution or replaceable rules.

    Content key: ChooseStructureSoleTraderCost

    Inline content: Setting up your business will cost: $35+ $479+

    Help content:

    What is the cost of setting up?

    Cost of setting up a sole trader

    The cost of setting up a sole trader depends on the type of business you want to set up. Sole trader start-up costs can include:

    • Registering a business name (if you need one) - $34 for one year or $80 for three years.
    • Licences and permits (you may not need any ? this depends on the industry you're in)
    • Domain name and web hosting (if you want a business website)

    See our Start-up costing sheet for examples of other costs that are common in the first year of a business.

    Cost of setting up a company

    The cost of setting up a company is much higher compared to a sole trader. Company start-up costs include:

    • Company registration - $479 for a proprietary limited company. With an annual review fee of $254.
    • Business name registration (if you need one) - $35 for one year or $82 for three years.
    • Licences and permits (you may not need any ? this depends on the industry you're in).
    • Domain name and web hosting (if you want your business to have a website).

    See our Start-up costing sheet for a list of examples of some of the other costs typical in the first year of a business.

    Content key: ChooseStructureSoleTraderHowEasy

    Inline content: Setting up your business will be: Easy More complex

    Help content:

    How easy is it to set up?

    Setting up as a sole trader

    Setting up a sole trader structure is relatively easy. Generally, you?re ready to go once you get your Australian Business Number (ABN) and register for any taxes, licences and permits. It?s recommended you set up a separate bank account, but it?s not necessary.

    Setting up as a company

    Setting up a company is more complicated, and you must understand your legal and tax obligations before registering. On top of the typical business and tax registrations, licences and permits you'll also need to set up a separate bank account and ensure the company has a registered office and principal place of business.

    Find out more about the volume of paperwork and legal requirements for a company.

    Content key: ChooseStructureSoleTraderSwitch

    Inline content: Switching business structures will be: Easy More difficult

    Help content:

    Can I switch business structures easily?

    A sole trader can easily switch to a partnership or company.

    A company can?t easily change business structures. To change, you need to:

    1. deregister the company so that it is no longer a legal entity. To do this, you either voluntarily deregister the company (if it has net assets worth less than $1000) or wind up the company (if it has net assets worth more than $1000). Winding up a company can be quite complicated
    2. register your business in the new structure.

    Content key: ChooseStructureSoleTraderMoney

    Inline content: The money earned belongs to: You Company

    Help content:

    Who owns the money earned?

    As a sole trader you own the money earned through the business. The money is treated as income on your personal tax return. You also own losses.

    In a company structure, the money belongs to the company. There are rules about how money is transferred to directors and shareholders. The money must be kept in a separate bank account and can?t be used for personal expenses. All money flowing in and out of a company must be documented and properly accounted for.

    Seek professional advice from a lawyer, accountant or financial adviser to find out more about handling earnings and the rules around how companies can transfer money.

    Content key: ChooseStructureSoleTraderLosses

    Inline content: Losses are the responsibility of: Sole trader Company & directors

    Help content:

    Who is responsible for losses?

    As a sole trader you are personally responsible for all payments, taxes, liabilities and losses. Your personal assets can be used to help pay for any business debts.

    In a company structure, the company is responsible for all payments, taxes, liabilities and losses. The personal assets of directors and shareholders are separate from the company, so they can?t be used to pay company debts. In certain cases where directors breach their duties, they may be held personally responsible for company losses. Find out who is liable for business debts and what that means for your business.

    Content key: ChooseStructureSoleTraderRisk

    Inline content: The risk to my personal assets are: High Low (Shareholders) to Medium (Directors)

    Help content:

    Risk to personal assets?

    As a sole trader, your liability is unlimited. This means all personal assets in your name can be used to pay business debts including if the business is sued. Find out more about who is liable for business debts.

    In a company structure, shareholders' personal assets aren't at risk. However, directors may be personally liable for company losses in certain circumstances or where they have breached their duties.

    Content key: ChooseStructureSoleTraderTaxRate

    Inline content: The tax rate that applies to income is: Individual tax rate 30%, Small business (27.5%)

    Help content:

    Tax rate?

    As a sole trader, all the money your business earns is treated as personal income on your tax return. All of your income, including money earned through your business, will be taxed at your individual tax rate. If your total income is $18,200 or less, you don?t pay any tax. This is called the ?tax-free threshold?. Anything above that amount will be taxed according to the amount of money you have earned.

    Work out your tax rate from our How is tax calculated? page.

    Companies with a turnover of less than $10 million are taxed at 27.5%. All other companies are taxed at 30%. There is no tax-free threshold for a company business structure. Work out your tax rate from our How is tax calculated? page.

    Content key: ChooseStructurePartnershipDecisions

    Inline content: Business decisions are made by: Partners Directors

    Help content:

    Who makes business decisions?

    In a partnership structure, all partners share the business decisions equally. If a ?formal partnership agreement? or legal agreement is drawn up, decisions must follow what was agreed to in the partnership agreement.

    In a company, the directors are responsible for business decisions and must make a record of certain decisions. If you?re the only director, you have full control.

    If there is more than one director, the way the company will be run (including how decisions are made) will follow certain rules. These rules are usually set out in the company's constitution or replaceable rules.

    Content key: ChooseStructurePartnershipCost

    Inline content: Setting up your business will cost: $35+ $479+

    Help content:

    What is the cost of setting up?

    Cost of setting up a partnership

    The cost of setting up a partnership depends on the type of business you want to set up. Partnership set up costs can include:

    • Registering a business name (if you need one) - one year ($35) or three years ($82)
    • Formal partnership agreement (if the partners want one)
    • Licences and permits (if applicable)
    • Domain name and web hosting (if you want a business website).

    See our Start-up costing sheet for examples of other costs that are common in the first year of a business.

    Cost of setting up a company

    The cost of setting up a company is much higher compared to a sole trader. Company set up costs:

    • Company registration?-?$479 for a proprietary limited company. With an annual review fee of $254.
    • Business name registration (if you need one) - $35 for one year or $82 for three years.
    • Licences and permits (if applicable ? this depends on your industry)
    • Domain name and web hosting (if you want your business to have a website).

    See our Start-up costing sheet for examples of other costs that are common in the first year of business.

    Content key: ChooseStructurePartnershipHowEasy

    Inline content: Setting up your business will be: Easy More complex

    Help content:

    How easy is it to set up?

    Setting up a partnership structure is relatively easy. On top of the typical business and tax registrations, and licences and permits, you will also need to set up a separate bank account.

    Setting up a company is more complicated, and you must understand your legal and tax obligations before registering. On top of the typical business and tax registrations, licences and permits you'll also need to set up a separate bank account and ensure the company has a registered office and principal place of business.

    Find out more about the volume of paperwork and legal requirements for a company.

    Content key: ChooseStructurePartnershipSwitch

    Inline content: Switching business structures will be: Easy More difficult

    Help content:

    Can I switch business structures easily?

    Partnerships can generally switch to a different business structure quite easily, as long as all partners agree.

    A company can?t easily change business structures. To change, you need to:

    1. deregister the company so that it is no longer a legal entity. To do this, you either voluntarily deregister the company (if it has net assets worth less than $1000) or wind up the company (if it has net assets worth more than $1000). Winding up a company can be quite complicated
    2. register your business in the new structure.

    Content key: ChooseStructurePartnershipMoney

    Inline content: The money earned belongs to: Partners Company

    Help content:

    Who owns the money earned?

    In a partnership, the money earned is usually shared among the partners equally. Partners also equally own losses. If a ?formal partnership agreement? or legal agreement is drawn up, earnings and losses must be shared according to what was agreed to.

    In a company structure, the money belongs to the company. There are rules about how money is transferred to directors and shareholders. The money must be kept in a separate bank account and can?t be used for personal expenses. All money flowing in and out of a company must be documented and properly accounted for.

    Seek professional advice from a lawyer, accountant or financial adviser to find out more about handling earnings and the rules around how companies can transfer money.

    Content key: ChooseStructurePartnershipLosses

    Inline content: Losses are the responsibility of: Partners Company & directors

    Help content:

    Who is responsible for losses?

    Partners keep their share of income earned through the business, but are also personally responsible for all payments, taxes, liabilities and losses. The personal assets of partners can also be used to help pay for any business debts. Partners who have an unequal share of the partnership can have a ?formal partnership agreement? written up to detail the percentage of income paid to each partner as well as the percentage of losses each partner must pay.

    In a company structure, the company pays for all payments, taxes, liabilities and losses. As a director and shareholder your personal assets are separate from the company so can?t be used to pay company debts. In certain cases where directors breach their duties, they may be held personally responsible for company losses. Discover more about who is liable for business debts and what that means for your business.

    Content key: ChooseStructurePartnershipRisk

    Inline content: The risk to my personal assets are: High Low (Shareholders) to Medium (Directors)

    Help content:

    Risk to personal assets?

    The liability of each partner is unlimited. This means all personal assets in your name can be used to pay business debts including if the business is sued. Your home and other assets could potentially be used to pay off the debt, even if the debt was incurred by another partner.

    A limited partnership structure can limit the risk to assets in certain cases, for example to protect silent partners who don?t make decisions or run the business. Find out more about who is liable for business debts.

    As a shareholder of a company there is no risk to your personal assets. However, directors may be personally liable for company losses in certain circumstances or where they have breached their duties.

    Content key: ChooseStructurePartnershipTaxRate

    Inline content: The tax rate that applies to income is: Individual tax rate 30%, Small business (27.5%)

    Help content:

    Tax rate?

    As a partner in a partnership, all the money you earn from the partnership is treated as personal income on your individual tax return. All of your income including money earned through your business will be taxed at your individual tax rate. If your total income is $18,200 or less, you don?t pay any tax. This is called the ?tax-free threshold?. Anything above that amount will be taxed according to the amount of money you have earned. Although the partnership must also lodge a tax return, the partnership doesn?t pay tax separately.

    Work out your tax rate from our How is tax calculated? page.

    Companies with a turnover of less than $10 million are taxed at 27.5%. All other companies are taxed at 30%. There is no tax-free threshold for a company business structure. To work out your tax rate, see our How is tax calculated page.

    Content key: BusinessAssociateResidentialAddress

    Help content:

    Residential address

    Residential address means the address that the individual normally lives at for most of the time.

    The residential address of the following business associates will be available to the public in the company register:

    • individual shareholders in the top 20 of each share class (based on shareholdings)
    • officeholders (directors and secretaries).

    If an officeholder has approval from Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC) to use an address that's different to their residential address, enter it here. Later in the form, we'll ask you to provide the ASIC approval number.

    Officeholders can apply for approval to provide a different address in some circumstances. For example, if providing the residential address would risk the safety of the officeholder or their family.

    Visit the ASIC website for information about the application fee and how to apply for suppression of residential address.

    Read the summary of ASIC's privacy policy to find out how ASIC handles personal information.

    Content key: CompanyNameHelp

    Help content:

    Company name format

    When you enter your company name, it will automatically be converted to uppercase letters. The system will also accept the following:

    • spaces
    • letters A to Z
    • numbers 0 to 9
    • standard punctuation marks such as . , ? ! ( ) { } : ; ' ? ?
    • the symbols | - _ \ / @ # $ % * = &

    Letters with an accent are not accepted. For example: à, é, ĉ or ç.

    For example: Lisa wants to register 'Lisa's Café Ltd'.

    Although her name will need to be registered as 'LISA'S CAFE LTD', she can still use 'Lisa's Café Ltd' on her marketing, signage and receipts.

    Legal element

    All company names have a legal element to identify the company type.

    Proprietary limited companies

    You can choose 'Proprietary Limited' or a shortened version of it, such as 'Pty Ltd'.

    Public companies limited by shares or guarantee

    You can choose 'Limited' or a shortened version of it, such as 'Ltd'.

    The company can choose to not have any legal elements in the company name if it meets requirements (see Section 150 of the Corporations Act 2001 on the Legislation Register website).

    No liability companies

    You can choose 'No liability' or a shortened version of it, such as 'NL'.

    Content key: CompanyDetailsBusinessActivityOrganisationSubTypeCode

    Help content:

    Organisation subtype

    Corporate limited partnership

    A corporate limited partnership or incorporated limited partnership is a type of limited partnership where the partnership exists as a separate legal entity to the partners.

    Your corporate limited partnership needs to be registered with the relevant Consumer affairs or Fair trading office in your state or territory.

    Corporate limited partnerships must:

    • report as a company for income tax purposes
    • lodge a company tax return instead of a partnership return.

    To find out if you're a corporate limited partnership for tax purposes, visit the Australian Taxation Office website.

    If you're not sure about something to do with your corporate limited partnership, please seek independent legal advice.

    Closely held trust

    Generally, a trust is a closely held trust if it either:

    • meets the '20/75 test', or
    • is a discretionary trust (one that is not a fixed trust).

    A trust meets the 20/75 test if up to 20 individuals have fixed entitlements that make up 75 per cent or more of the income or capital of the trust.

    Read more detail about the rules for closely held trusts on the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) website.

    If you're not sure about detailed aspects of your trust, please seek independent legal advice.

    Content key: CompanyDetailsUnincorporatedMembersHeading

    Inline content: Representative of organisation

    Content key: AUSkey

    Help content:

    Business logins - AUSkey

    Only Administrator AUSkey registration available here. To register a tax agent or accountant for a Standard AUSkey, you'll need to register on the Australian Business Register (ABR) website.

    What is an AUSkey?

    An AUSkey is a secure login that's set up on your computer. If you're eligible, AUSkey lets you log in to many government services including business and tax forms.

    What can I do with an AUSkey?

    With a compatible system or browser, an AUSkey can help you:

    • update your business name details through ASIC connect
    • lodge your Business Activity Statements (BAS) through the Business Portal
    • update your Australian Business Number (ABN) details
    • submit tax statements through 'SBR-enabled' accounting, bookkeeping or payroll desktop software.

    See where you can use your AUSkey on the Australian Business Register (ABR) website.

    If you want to log in from different computers, you can set up an AUSkey on a USB stick.

    How do I register?

    You can only register for an Administrator AUSkey here by selecting the AUSkey checkbox together with another registration on the 'Ready to register' screen. All you need to provide is your:

    • Tax file number
    • Australian telephone number
    • Email address.

    Find out more about registering for an AUSkey on the ABR website.

    You can now choose how you want to log in to manage your government business and tax details. Only AUSkey is available to register here on this Business registration form. To find out more about additional business login options, expand the "Additional logins" tab below.

    Additional logins

    You can now choose how you want to log in to manage your government business and tax details.

    You can use your AUSkey or one of the login options below:

    • Manage ABN connections
      If you have a myGov login, you can link your ABNs to your myGov account. This means you can then use your myGov account to manage your ABN details.
    • Online or 'cloud' tax software
      You can now use eligible online or 'cloud' accounting, bookkeeping or payroll software to process certain government tax forms.

    Please note: the login options above are for specific tax paperwork. You'll still need to use either your AUSkey or ASIC connect login to:

    • manage business name details or renewals
    • submit tax forms through 'SBR-enabled' accounting, bookkeeping or payroll desktop software.

    Visit the myGov website to find out more about the Manage ABN Connections option.

    Find out more about the online or 'cloud' software service on the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) website.

    The Australian Government Business Registration Service is committed to providing accessible websites to the widest possible audience, regardless of technology or ability. Accessibility and usability have been key elements considered in the development of our website.     As per the guidelines set by the Web Accessibility National Transition Strategy, all government websites are required to meet the Web Content Accessibility Guidelines version 2.0 (WCAG 2.0 Double A compliance). The Business Registration Service aims to conform to the Double A standard of the WCAG 2.0, as developed by the World Wide Web Consortium (W3C).

    Navigating our site

    Our website has been designed to responsively change based on the device you are using to access it. This means that you can access the site using a desktop browser, tablet or mobile device and the website layout will change to make it easier to use.

    Our accessibility goals

    We are committed to:

    • improving access to business and tax registration forms to ensure they are available to all businesses
    • considering the needs of people with disabilities in the development of our online products and services
    • writing our help in plain and simple language so that it's easy to understand for as many people as possible.

    Contact us for help or provide your feedback

    Accessibility is very important to us. Please email us if you have any difficulty in accessing our form. We are trying to make this website as accessible as possible, so please let us know if you think there's anything we can improve. Share your thoughts with us on our feedback form.

    The National Relay Service

    If you need assistance when contacting us via phone, the National Relay Service can help. The National Relay Service helps people throughout Australia who are deaf or have a hearing or speech impairment to telephone the wider community through a third person. The National Relay Service is a standard call and operates 24 hours a day, every day of the year.

    Visit the National Relay Service for more information.

    Content key: LegalNoticesPrivacy

    Inline content:

    The Australian Government Business Registration Service website is managed by the Department of Industry, Innovation and Science. We respect and protect the privacy of our users. This privacy policy tells you how we collect and use information.

    The Department of Industry, Innovation and Science is bound by the Australian Privacy Principles (APPs) under the Privacy Act 1988 with respect to collection, use and disclosure of personal information. The Business Registration Service follows the Department of Industry, Innovation and Science's Privacy Policy and the APP Guidelines provided by the Office of the Australian Information Commissioner.

    Purpose of the Business Registration Service

    The Business Registration Service securely transfers applicant details to the Australian Taxation Office (ATO) and the Australian Securities & Investments Commission (ASIC) for a number of business and tax registration applications.

    This service is in addition to the services offered by the ATO Australian Business Register (ABR) and ASIC.

    The Australian Business Registration Service does not administer or maintain the information on the ABN, company and business names registers. ATO and ASIC are responsible for maintaining and administering register information.

    Why we collect information

    We only collect personal information that is necessary for, or directly related to, one or more of our business or tax registrations:

    • Australian company
    • Australian business name
    • Australian Business Number (ABN)
    • Goods and Services Tax (GST)
    • Pay as You Go withholding (PAYGw)
    • Fringe Benefits Tax (FBT)
    • Luxury Car Tax (LCT)
    • Fuel Tax Credits (FTC)
    • Wine Equalisation Tax (WET)
    • AUSkey
    • Save and return login.

    We collect personal information from individuals or their authorised representatives. We collect this information to:

    • provide to the ATO or ASIC through APIs so they can process your business or tax registration applications
    • provide your payment information to ASIC through APIs so they can process your payment
    • check whether your proposed company or business name is available
    • save your details so you can complete your application at a later date.

    The Business Registration Service does not collect personal information from other Government agencies. However, once we have forwarded your information to ATO and ASIC, these agencies may collect other personal information from other agencies. In that case, the ATO and ASIC will inform you.

    Once your details are forwarded to the ATO and ASIC, certain information such as business name, company name, and ACN and ABN details will be made available to the public on the ABN Lookup website and the ASIC Connect name registers.

    For more about how your information is collected and protected by these agencies, see the legal statements below:

    • ABR privacy statement
    • ATO privacy policy
    • AUSkey privacy statement
    • ASIC privacy policy

    Visiting our website

    When you browse our website, our service provider logs the following information for statistical purposes - your server address, top level domain name (eg .com, .gov, .au, .uk), the date and time of your visit, the pages accessed, entity type selected, the previous site visited and the type of browser used.

    We do not identify users or their browsing activities except, in the event of an investigation, where a law enforcement agency may be entitled to inspect the service provider's logs.

    From time to time we may use cookies on our website to help us carry out online surveys. Cookies are small pieces of information exchanged between your web browser and a website server. Where we use an external survey provider, that provider could use cookies on their website. If this is the case you will be directed to information on the provider's website explaining the use of cookies.

    Access and correction

    The Privacy Act allows individuals to seek access to and request correction of records containing their personal information. The Freedom of Information Act 1982 also sets out the process by which you can access, change or annotate records held by us, which contain your personal information.

    The Business Registration Service stores form data securely until the form data is lodged with ATO and ASIC and the application process is completed. If the form has not been submitted, the form data will be stored for 30 days from the last login.

    The information is then deleted and only information related to the lodgement status of your applications will remain on the Business Registration Service system.

    To seek access or request a correction to your personal information once a form is lodged, please see the privacy statements below to understand each agency's process:

    • ABR privacy statement
    • ATO privacy policy
    • AUSkey privacy statement
    • ASIC privacy policy

    Complaints

    All general enquiries, complaints about the Department's compliance with the Privacy Act, or requests for access or correction, should be directed to the Department's Privacy Contact Officer.

    Email: privacy@industry.gov.au

    Post: Privacy Contact Officer

    Content key: LegalNoticesSecurity

    Inline content:

    The Department of Industry, Innovation and Science (DIIS) operates the Australian Government Business Registration Service ('Service'). The Service passes information to and from the Australian Taxation Office (ATO), the Australian Business Register (ABR) and the Australian Securities and Investments Commission (ASIC) Business Name registration systems through Application Programming Interfaces (APIs).

    We use a range of security controls to protect the Service from unauthorised access, and to protect information while it is collected by, stored or passed through to the ATO, ABR and ASIC systems.

    The Crimes Act 1914 and the Privacy Act 1988 respectively governs Australian Government agencies and their use and disclosure of information. There are severe penalties for breaches of the provisions contained in these Acts. We will not release personal information unless the law permits it or you give us permission.

    The Business Registration Service operates in a secure environment and a reliable system but you should be aware that there may be risks associated with the transmission of information through the Internet.

    For more information you can read our privacy statement or contact us.

    Confidentiality and integrity

    We take steps to help ensure the confidentiality and integrity of the data passed to and from the Service. The Service has two forms of protection:

    • TLS (Transport Layer Security) or 'https': encryption, which provides the secure connection between the user and the Business Registration Service server. Users seeking more information about TLS can consult the W3C 'The world wide web consortium' website.
    • Claims-Based Identity is used to verify that the user is who they claim to be which helps ensure the security of electronic transactions.

    User awareness of location

    You can confirm you're interacting with the Department of Industry, Innovation and Science by checking the digital certificate used to provide SSL encryption. You can check this by clicking on the SSL padlock located along the top of the browser window. You should confirm that the certificate:

    • has been issued to 'register.business.gov.au'
    • has been issued by 'Symantec Class 3 EV SSL CA'
    • has a validity period of two years
    • path/hierarchy shows only 'Symantec Class 3 EV SSL CA' followed by 'register.business.gov.au'.

    How these details are displayed depends on the type of browser being used.

    Storage of information

    The Business Registration Service stores form data securely until the form data is lodged with ATO and ASIC and the application process is completed. If the form has not been submitted, the form data will be stored for 30 days from the last login.

    Online security

    We take the security and privacy of your personal information seriously, and have a range of systems and controls to ensure that your information and transactions with us are safe.

    We will never ask you for your login details, Tax File Number (TFN), bank details or other personal identification information in an electronic communication to you (including email or text message).

    Unlisted

    An unlisted company is a company that's not included in the official list of a financial or share market (such as the ASX).

    An unlisted company can decide to become listed in the future, if it meets the requirements of the financial or share market.

    A company needs to go through the listing process if it wants its shares to be traded by the public on a financial or share market.

    Read more about going public (listing on a securities exchange) on the Australian Securities & Investments Commission website.

    Content key: PartnerJointVentureName

    Help content:

    Legal name of partnership

    A partnership's legal name is made up of the names of all the partners in the partnership. The names should be:

    • in alphabetical order
    • separated by '&'
    • include the initials of the first names and the full last name.

    For example, A Baker & G Caruthers & D F Smith.

    Content key: HelpMeDecideEmployeesSoletraderQuestion

    Inline content: Will you take on employees?

    Content key: HelpMeDecideEmployeesSoletraderInformation

    Inline content: Independent contractors, sub-contractors and volunteers are not considered employees. @HelpLink

    Help content:

    Employees

    Generally, employees:

    • can be casual, permanent, part-time or full-time
    • are supervised by an employer
    • are required to carry out their work in a particular way
    • earn a wage or salary
    • are entitled to paid leave
    • work set hours on a permanent, fixed term or casual basis
    • can't subcontract work tasks given to them.

    Don't confuse employees with contractors! Contractors set up businesses to hire out their services to other businesses, and can negotiate their own working arrangements.

    If you're not sure whether you'll be taking on an employee or a contractor, try our Independent contractors decision tool.

    Content key: HelpMeDecideFringeBenefitsSoletraderQuestion

    Inline content: Will you offer fringe benefits to your employees?

    Content key: HelpMeDecideFringeBenefitsSoletraderInformation

    Inline content: Fringe benefits are non-cash benefits that employers offer to employees, such as a work car for private use, low-interest loans, or movie tickets. @HelpLink

    Help content:

    Fringe Benefits Tax (FBT)

    As an employer, if you give certain fringe benefits to your employees, you must pay tax on the value of the fringe benefits.

    What do I need to do?

    Being registered for FBT means you:

    • work out how much FBT you have to pay
    • keep records related to the fringe benefits you provide
    • report fringe benefits on your employees' payment summaries
    • report fringe benefits to the Australian Taxation Office (ATO)
    • pay the FBT to the ATO.

    Find out more

    Read about FBT on the ATO website.
